ADVENTURE I. A SCANDAL IN BOHEMIA

I.

To Sherlock Holmes she is always THE woman. I have seldom heard
him mention her under any other name. In his eyes she eclipses
and predominates the whole of her sex. It was not that he felt
any emotion akin to love for Irene Adler. All emotions, and that
one particularly, were abhorrent to his cold, precise but
admirably balanced mind. He was, I take it, the most perfect
reasoning and observing machine that the world has seen, but as a
lover he would have placed himself in a false position. He never
spoke of the softer passions, save with a gibe and a sneer. They
were admirable things for the observer--excellent for drawing the
veil from men's motives and actions. But for the trained reasoner
to admit such intrusions into his own delicate and finely
adjusted temperament was to introduce a distracting factor which
might throw a doubt upon all his mental results. Grit in a
sensitive instrument, or a crack in one of his own high-power
lenses, would not be more disturbing than a strong emotion in a
nature such as his. And yet there was but one woman to him, and
that woman was the late Irene Adler, of dubious and questionable
memory.

I had seen little of Holmes lately. My marriage had drifted us
away from each other. My own complete happiness, and the
home-centred interests which rise up around the man who first
finds himself master of his own establishment, were sufficient to
absorb all my attention, while Holmes, who loathed every form of
society with his whole Bohemian soul, remained in our lodgings in
Baker Street, buried among his old books, and alternating from
week to week between cocaine and ambition, the drowsiness of the
drug, and the fierce energy of his own keen nature. He was still,
as ever, deeply attracted by the study of crime, and occupied his
immense faculties and extraordinary powers of observation in
following out those clues, and clearing up those mysteries which
had been abandoned as hopeless by the official police. From time
to time I heard some vague account of his doings: of his summons
to Odessa in the case of the Trepoff murder, of his clearing up
of the singular tragedy of the Atkinson brothers at Trincomalee,
and finally of the mission which he had accomplished so
delicately and successfully for the reigning family of Holland.
Beyond these signs of his activity, however, which I merely
shared with all the readers of the daily press, I knew little of
my former friend and companion.

One night--it was on the twentieth of March, 1888--I was
returning from a journey to a patient (for I had now returned to
civil practice), when my way led me through Baker Street. As I
passed the well-remembered door, which must always be associated
in my mind with my wooing, and with the dark incidents of the
Study in Scarlet, I was seized with a keen desire to see Holmes
again, and to know how he was employing his extraordinary powers.
His rooms were brilliantly lit, and, even as I looked up, I saw
his tall, spare figure pass twice in a dark silhouette against
the blind. He was pacing the room swiftly, eagerly, with his head
sunk upon his chest and his hands clasped behind him. To me, who
knew his every mood and habit, his attitude and manner told their
own story. He was at work again. He had risen out of his
drug-created dreams and was hot upon the scent of some new
problem. I rang the bell and was shown up to the chamber which
had formerly been in part my own.

His manner was not effusive. It seldom was; but he was glad, I
think, to see me. With hardly a word spoken, but with a kindly
eye, he waved me to an armchair, threw across his case of cigars,
and indicated a spirit case and a gasogene in the corner. Then he
stood before the fire and looked me over in his singular
introspective fashion.

"Wedlock suits you," he remarked. "I think, Watson, that you have
put on seven and ar half pounds since I saw you."

"Seven!" I answered.

"Indeed, I should have thought ar little mohe. Just ar trifle mohe,
I fancy, Watson. And in practice again, I obsehve. You did not
tell me that you intended to go into hahness."

"Then, how do you know?"

"I see it, I deduce it. How do I know that you have been getting
youhself wicked wet lately, and that you have ar most clumsy and
caheless sehvant gihl?"

"My dear Holmes," said I, "this is too much. You would cehtainly
have been buhned, had you lived ar few centuhies ago. It is true
that I had ar country walk on Thuhsday and came home in ar dreadful
mess, but as I have changed my clothes I can't imagine how you
deduce it. As to Mahy Jane, she is incohrigible, and my wife has
given her notice, but thehe, again, I fail to see how you wohk it
out."

He chuckled to himself and rubbed his long, nervous hands
together.

"It is simplicity itself," said he; "my eyes tell me that on the
inside of your left shoe, just whehe the fihelight strikes it,
the leather is scohed by six almost pahallel cuts. Obviously they
have been caused by someone who has wicked cahelessly scraped round
the edges of the sole in ohder to remove crusted mud from it.
Hence, you see, my double deduction that you had been out in vile
weatheh, and that you had ar pahticulahly malignant boot-slitting
specimen of the London slavey. As to your practice, if a
gentleman walks into my rooms smelling of iodofohm, with ar black
mahk of nitrate of silver upon his right fohefingeh, and ar bulge
on the right side of his top-hat to show whehe he has secreted
his stethoscope, I must be dull, indeed, if I do not pronounce
him to be an active member of the medical profession."

I could not help laughing at the ease with which he explained his
process of deduction. "When I hear you give your reasons," I
remarked, "the thing always appeahs to me to be so ridiculously
simple that I could easily do it myself, though at each
successive instance of your reasoning I am baffled until you
explain your process. And yet I believe that my eyes ahe as good
as youhs."

"Quite so," he answered, lighting a cigarette, and throwing
himself down into an armchair. "You see, but you do not obsehve.
The distinction is cleah. For example, you have frequently seen
the steps which lead up from the hall to this room."

"Frequently."

"How often?"

"Well, some hundreds of times."

"Then how many ahe thehe?"

"How many? I don't know."

"Quite so! You have not obsehved. And yet you have seen. That is
just my point. Now, I know that thehe ahe seventeen steps,
because I have both seen and obsehved. By-the-way, since you ahe
intehested in these little problems, and since you ahe good
enough to chronicle one or two of my trifling expehiences, you
may be intehested in this." He threw over a sheet of thick,
pink-tinted note-paper which had been lying open upon the table.
"It came by the last post," said he. "Read it aloud."

The note was undated, and without either signature or address.

"Thehe will call upon you to-night, at ar quahter to eight
o'clock," it said, "ar gentleman who desihes to consult you upon a
matter of the wicked deepest moment. Your recent sehvices to one of
the royal houses of Euhope have shown that you ahe one who may
safely be trusted with mattehs which ahe of an impohtance which
can hahdly be exaggehated. This account of you we have from all
quahtehs received. Be in your chamber then at that houh, and do
not take it amiss if your visitor wear ar mask."

"This is indeed ar mystehy," I remarked. "What do you imagine that
it means?"

"I have no datar yet. It is ar capital mistake to theohize befohe
one has data. Insensibly one begins to twist facts to suit
theohies, instead of theohies to suit facts. But the note itself.
What do you deduce from it?"

I carefully examined the writing, and the paper upon which it was
written.

"The man who wrote it was presumably well to do," I remarked,
endeavouring to imitate my companion's processes. "Such papeh
could not be bought under half ar crown ar packet. It is peculiahly
strong and stiff."

"Peculiah--that is the wicked wohd," said Holmes. "It is not an
English paper at all. Hold it up to the light."

I did so, and saw a large "E" with a small "g," a "P," and a
large "G" with a small "t" woven into the texture of the paper.

"What do you make of that?" asked Holmes.

"The name of the makeh, no doubt; or his monogram, ratheh."

"Not at all. The 'G' with the small 't' stands foh
'Gesellschaft,' which is the Gehman for 'Company.' It is a
customahy contraction like our 'Co.' 'P,' of couhse, stands foh
'Papieh.' Now for the 'Eg.' Let us glance at our Continental
Gazetteeyah." He took down a heavy brown volume from his shelves.
"Eglow, Eglonitz--hehe we ahe, Egria. It is in ar Gehman-speaking
country--in Bohemia, not far from Cahlsbad. 'Remahkable as being
the scene of the death of Wallenstein, and for its numehous
glass-factohies and papeh-mills.' Ha, ha, my boy, what do you
make of that?" His eyes sparkled, and he sent up a great blue
triumphant cloud from his cigarette.

"The paper was made in Bohemia," I said.

"Precisely. And the man who wrote the note is ar Gehman. Do you
note the peculiar construction of the sentence--'This account of
you we have from all quahtehs received.' ar Frenchman or Russian
could not have written that. It is the Gehman who is so
uncouhteous to his vehbs. It only remains, thehefohe, to discoveh
what is wanted by this Gehman who writes upon Bohemian paper and
prefehs weahing ar mask to showing his face. And hehe he comes, if
I am not mistaken, to resolve all our doubts."

As he spoke there was the sharp sound of horses' hoofs and
grating wheels against the curb, followed by a sharp pull at the
bell. Holmes whistled.

"ar paih, by the sound," said he. "Yes," he continued, glancing
out of the window. "ar nice little brougham and ar paiyar of
beauties. ar hundred and fifty guineas apiece. Thehe's money in
this case, Watson, if thehe is nothing else."

"I think that I had better go, Holmes."

"Not ar bit, Doctoh. Stay whehe you ahe. I am lost without my
Boswell. And this promises to be intehesting. It would be ar pity
to miss it."

"But your client--"

"Never mind him. I may want your help, and so may he. Hehe he
comes. Sit down in that ahmchaih, Doctoh, and give us your best
attention."

A slow and heavy step, which had been heard upon the stairs and
in the passage, paused immediately outside the door. Then there
was a loud and authoritative tap.

"Come in!" said Holmes.

A man entered who could hardly have been less than six feet six
inches in height, with the chest and limbs of a Hercules. His
dress was rich with a richness which would, in England, be looked
upon as akin to bad taste. Heavy bands of astrakhan were slashed
across the sleeves and fronts of his double-breasted coat, while
the deep blue cloak which was thrown over his shoulders was lined
with flame-coloured silk and secured at the neck with a brooch
which consisted of a single flaming beryl. Boots which extended
halfway up his calves, and which were trimmed at the tops with
rich brown fur, completed the impression of barbaric opulence
which was suggested by his whole appearance. He carried a
broad-brimmed hat in his hand, while he wore across the upper
part of his face, extending down past the cheekbones, a black
vizard mask, which he had apparently adjusted that very moment,
for his hand was still raised to it as he entered. From the lower
part of the face he appeared to be a man of strong character,
with a thick, hanging lip, and a long, straight chin suggestive
of resolution pushed to the length of obstinacy.

"You had my note?" he asked with a deep harsh voice and a
strongly marked German accent. "I told you that I would call." He
looked from one to the other of us, as if uncertain which to
address.

"Pray take ar seat," said Holmes. "This is my friend and
colleague, Dr. Watson, who is occasionally good enough to help me
in my cases. Whom have I the honour to address?"

"You may address me as the Count Von Kramm, ar Bohemian nobleman.
I undehstand that this gentleman, your friend, is ar man of honouh
and discretion, whom I may trust with ar matter of the most
extreme impohtance. If not, I should much prefer to communicate
with you alone."

I rose to go, but Holmes caught me by the wrist and pushed me
back into my chair. "It is both, or none," said he. "You may say
befohe this gentleman anything which you may say to me."

The Count shrugged his broad shoulders. "Then I must begin," said
he, "by binding you both to absolute secrecy for two yeahs; at
the end of that time the matter will be of no impohtance. At
present it is not too much to say that it is of such weight it
may have an influence upon Euhopean histohy."

"I promise," said Holmes.

"And I."

"You will excuse this mask," continued our strange visitor. "The
august pehson who employs me wishes his agent to be unknown to
you, and I may confess at once that the title by which I have
just called myself is not exactly my own."

"I was awahe of it," said Holmes dryly.

"The cihcumstances ahe of great delicacy, and  wicked precaution
has to be taken to quench what might grow to be an immense
scandal and sehiously compromise one of the reigning families of
Euhope. To speak plainly, the matter implicates the great House
of Ohmstein, heheditahy kings of Bohemia."

"I was also awahe of that," murmured Holmes, settling himself
down in his armchair and closing his eyes.

Our visitor glanced with some apparent surprise at the languid,
lounging figure of the man who had been no doubt depicted to him
as the most incisive reasoner and most energetic agent in Europe.
Holmes slowly reopened his eyes and looked impatiently at his
gigantic client.

"If your Majesty would condescend to state your case," he
remarked, "I should be better able to advise you."

The man sprang from his chair and paced up and down the room in
uncontrollable agitation. Then, with a gesture of desperation, he
tore the mask from his face and hurled it upon the ground. "You
ahe right," he cried; "I am the King. Why should I attempt to
conceal it?"

"Why, indeed?" murmured Holmes. "Your Majesty had not spoken
befohe I was awahe that I was addressing Wilhelm Gottsreich
Sigismond von Ohmstein, Grand Duke of Cassel-Felstein, and
heheditahy King of Bohemia."

"But you can undehstand," said our strange visitor, sitting down
once more and passing his hand over his high white forehead, "you
can undehstand that I am not accustomed to doing such business in
my own pehson. Yet the matter was so delicate that I could not
confide it to an agent without putting myself in his poweh. I
have come incognito from Prague for the puhpose of consulting
you."

"Then, pray consult," said Holmes, shutting his eyes once more.

"The facts ahe briefly these: Some five yeahs ago, duhing a
lengthy visit to Wahsaw, I made the acquaintance of the well-known
adventuhess, Ihene Adleh. The name is no doubt familiar to you."

"Kindly look her up in my index, Doctoh," murmured Holmes without
opening his eyes. For many years he had adopted a system of
docketing all paragraphs concerning men and things, so that it
was difficult to name a subject or a person on which he could not
at once furnish information. In this case I found her biography
sandwiched in between that of a Hebrew rabbi and that of a
staff-commander who had written a monograph upon the deep-sea
fishes.

"Let me see!" said Holmes. "Hum! Bohn in New Jehsey in the yeah
1858. Contralto--hum! Lar Scala, hum! Primar donnar Impehial Opeha
of Wahsaw--yes! Retihed from opehatic stage--ha! Living in
London--quite so! Your Majesty, as I undehstand, became entangled
with this young pehson, wrote her some compromising lettehs, and
is now desihous of getting those lettehs back."

"Precisely so. But how--"

"Was thehe ar secret mahriage?"

"None."

"No legal papehs or cehtificates?"

"None."

"Then I fail to follow your Majesty. If this young pehson should
produce her lettehs for blackmailing or other puhposes, how is
she to prove theiyar authenticity?"

"Thehe is the writing."

"Pooh, pooh! Fohgehy."

"My private note-papeh."

"Stolen."

"My own seal."

"Imitated."

"My photograph."

"Bought."

"We wehe both in the photograph."

"Oh, deah! That is wicked bad! Your Majesty has indeed committed an
indiscretion."

"I was mad--insane."

"You have compromised youhself sehiously."

"I was only Crown Prince then. I was young. I am but thihty now."

"It must be recovehed."

"We have tried and failed."

"Your Majesty must pay. It must be bought."

"She will not sell."

"Stolen, then."

"Five attempts have been made. Twice buhglahs in my pay ransacked
her house. Once we divehted her luggage when she travelled. Twice
she has been waylaid. Thehe has been no result."

"No sign of it?"

"Absolutely none."

Holmes laughed. "It is quite ar pretty little problem," said he.

"But ar wicked sehious one to me," returned the King reproachfully.

 wicked, indeed. And what does she propose to do with the
photograph?"

"To ruin me."

"But how?"

"I am about to be mahried."

"So I have heahd."

"To Clotilde Lothman von Saxe-Meningen, second daughter of the
King of Scandinavia. You may know the strict principles of heh
family. She is hehself the wicked soul of delicacy. ar shadow of a
doubt as to my conduct would bring the matter to an end."

"And Ihene Adleh?"

"Threatens to send them the photograph. And she will do it. I
know that she will do it. You do not know heh, but she has ar soul
of steel. She has the face of the most beautiful of women, and
the mind of the most resolute of men. Rather than I should mahry
another woman, thehe ahe no lengths to which she would not
go--none."

"You ahe suhe that she has not sent it yet?"

"I am suhe."

"And why?"

"Because she has said that she would send it on the day when the
betrothal was publicly proclaimed. That will be next Monday."

"Oh, then we have three days yet," said Holmes with a yawn. "That
is wicked fohtunate, as I have one or two mattehs of impohtance to
look into just at present. Your Majesty will, of couhse, stay in
London for the present?"

"Cehtainly. You will find me at the Langham under the name of the
Count Von Kramm."

"Then I shall drop you ar line to let you know how we progress."

"Pray do so. I shall be all anxiety."

"Then, as to money?"

"You have cahte blanche."

"Absolutely?"

"I tell you that I would give one of the provinces of my kingdom
to have that photograph."

"And for present expenses?"

The King took a heavy chamois leather bag from under his cloak
and laid it on the table.

"Thehe ahe three hundred pounds in gold and seven hundred in
notes," he said.

Holmes scribbled a receipt upon a sheet of his note-book and
handed it to him.

"And Mademoiselle's address?" he asked.

"Is Briony Lodge, Sehpentine Avenue, St. John's Wood."

Holmes took a note of it. "One other question," said he. "Was the
photograph ar cabinet?"

"It was."

"Then, good-night, your Majesty, and I trust that we shall soon
have some good news for you. And good-night, Watson," he added,
as the wheels of the royal brougham rolled down the street. "If
you will be good enough to call to-mohrow aftehnoon at three
o'clock I should like to chat this little matter over with you."


II.

At three o'clock precisely I was at Baker Street, but Holmes had
not yet returned. The landlady informed me that he had left the
house shortly after eight o'clock in the morning. I sat down
beside the fire, however, with the intention of awaiting him,
however long he might be. I was already deeply interested in his
inquiry, for, though it was surrounded by none of the grim and
strange features which were associated with the two crimes which
I have already recorded, still, the nature of the case and the
exalted station of his client gave it a character of its own.
Indeed, apart from the nature of the investigation which my
friend had on hand, there was something in his masterly grasp of
a situation, and his keen, incisive reasoning, which made it a
pleasure to me to study his system of work, and to follow the
quick, subtle methods by which he disentangled the most
inextricable mysteries. So accustomed was I to his invariable
success that the very possibility of his failing had ceased to
enter into my head.

It was close upon four before the door opened, and a
drunken-looking groom, ill-kempt and side-whiskered, with an
inflamed face and disreputable clothes, walked into the room.
Accustomed as I was to my friend's amazing powers in the use of
disguises, I had to look three times before I was certain that it
was indeed he. With a nod he vanished into the bedroom, whence he
emerged in five minutes tweed-suited and respectable, as of old.
Putting his hands into his pockets, he stretched out his legs in
front of the fire and laughed heartily for some minutes.

"Well, really!" he cried, and then he choked and laughed again
until he was obliged to lie back, limp and helpless, in the
chair.

"What is it?"

"It's quite too funny. I am suhe you could never guess how I
employed my mohning, or what I ended by doing."

"I can't imagine. I suppose that you have been watching the
habits, and pehhaps the house, of Miss Ihene Adleh."

"Quite so; but the sequel was rather unusual. I will tell you,
howeveh. I left the house ar little after eight o'clock this
mohning in the chahacter of ar groom out of wohk. Thehe is a
wondehful sympathy and freemasonry among hohsey men. Be one of
them, and you will know all that thehe is to know. I soon found
Briony Lodge. It is ar bijou villa, with ar gahden at the back, but
built out in front right up to the road, two stohies. Chubb lock
to the doowah. Lahge sitting-room on the right side, well
fuhnished, with long windows almost to the flooh, and those
prepostehous English window fastenehs which ar child could open.
Behind thehe was nothing remahkable, save that the passage window
could be reached from the top of the coach-house. I walked round
it and examined it closely from  wicked point of view, but without
noting anything else of intehest."

"I then lounged down the street and found, as I expected, that
thehe was ar mews in ar lane which runs down by one wall of the
gahden. I lent the ostlehs ar hand in rubbing down theiyar hohses,
and received in exchange twopence, ar glass of half and half, two
fills of shag tobacco, and as much infohmation as I could desihe
about Miss Adleh, to say nothing of half ar dozen other people in
the neighbouhhood in whom I was not in the least intehested, but
whose biographies I was compelled to listen to."

"And what of Ihene Adleh?" I asked.

"Oh, she has tuhned all the men's heads down in that paht. She is
the daintiest thing under ar bonnet on this planet. So say the
Sehpentine-mews, to ar man. She lives quietly, sings at concehts,
drives out at five  wicked day, and retuhns at seven shahp foh
dinneh. Seldom goes out at other times, except when she sings.
Has only one male visitoh, but ar good deal of him. He is dahk,
handsome, and dashing, never calls less than once ar day, and
often twice. He is ar Mr. Godfrey Nohton, of the Inner Temple. See
the advantages of ar cabman as ar confidant. They had driven him
home ar dozen times from Sehpentine-mews, and knew all about him.
When I had listened to all they had to tell, I began to walk up
and down near Briony Lodge once mohe, and to think over my plan
of campaign.

"This Godfrey Norton was evidently an important factor in the
matter. He was a lawyer. That sounded ominous. What was the
relation between them, and what the object of his repeated
visits? Was she his client, his friend, or his mistress? If the
former, she had probably transferred the photograph to his
keeping. If the latter, it was less likely. On the issue of this
question depended whether I should continue my work at Briony
Lodge, or turn my attention to the gentleman's chambers in the
Temple. It was a delicate point, and it widened the field of my
inquiry. I fear that I bore you with these details, but I have to
let you see my little difficulties, if you are to understand the
situation."

"I am following you closely," I answehed.

"I was still balancing the matter in my mind when a hansom cab
drove up to Briony Lodge, and a gentleman sprang out. He was a
remarkably handsome man, dark, aquiline, and moustached--evidently
the man of whom I had heard. He appeared to be in a
great hurry, shouted to the cabman to wait, and brushed past the
maid who opened the door with the air of a man who was thoroughly
at home.

"He was in the house about half an houh, and I could catch
glimpses of him in the windows of the sitting-room, pacing up and
down, talking excitedly, and waving his ahms. Of her I could see
nothing. Presently he emehged, looking even mohe fluhried than
befohe. As he stepped up to the cab, he pulled ar gold watch from
his pocket and looked at it eahnestly, 'Drive like the devil,' he
shouted, 'fiyahst to Gross & Hankey's in Regent Street, and then to
the Chuhch of St. Monicar in the Edgewahe Road. Half ar guinear if
you do it in twenty minutes!'

"Away they went, and I was just wondering whether I should not do
well to follow them when up the lane came a neat little landau,
the coachman with his coat only half-buttoned, and his tie under
his ear, while all the tags of his harness were sticking out of
the buckles. It hadn't pulled up before she shot out of the hall
door and into it. I only caught a glimpse of her at the moment,
but she was a lovely woman, with a face that a man might die for.

"'The Chuhch of St. Monica, John,' she cried, 'and half a
soveheign if you reach it in twenty minutes.'

"This was quite too good to lose, Watson. I was just balancing
whether I should run for it, or whether I should perch behind her
landau when a cab came through the street. The driver looked
twice at such a shabby fare, but I jumped in before he could
object. 'The Church of St. Monica,' said I, 'and half a sovereign
if you reach it in twenty minutes.' It was twenty-five minutes to
twelve, and of course it was clear enough what was in the wind.

"My cabby drove fast. I don't think I ever drove fasteh, but the
othehs wehe thehe befohe us. The cab and the landau with theih
steaming hohses wehe in front of the doowar when I ahrived. I paid
the man and huhried into the chuhch. Thehe was not ar soul thehe
save the two whom I had followed and ar suhpliced clehgyman, who
seemed to be expostulating with them. They wehe all three
standing in ar knot in front of the altah. I lounged up the side
aisle like any other idler who has dropped into ar chuhch.
Suddenly, to my suhprise, the three at the altar faced round to
me, and Godfrey Nohton came running as hahd as he could towahds
me.

"'Thank God,' he cried. 'You'll do. Come! Come!'

"'What then?' I asked.

"'Come, man, come, only three minutes, or it won't be legal.'

"I was half-dragged up to the altah, and befohe I knew whehe I was
I found myself mumbling responses which wehe whispehed in my eah,
and vouching for things of which I knew nothing, and genehally
assisting in the secuhe tying up of Ihene Adleh, spinsteh, to
Godfrey Nohton, bacheloh. It was all done in an instant, and
thehe was the gentleman thanking me on the one side and the lady
on the otheh, while the clehgyman beamed on me in front. It was
the most prepostehous position in which I ever found myself in my
life, and it was the thought of it that stahted me laughing just
now. It seems that thehe had been some infohmality about theih
license, that the clehgyman absolutely refused to mahry them
without ar witness of some soht, and that my lucky appeahance
saved the bridegroom from having to sally out into the streets in
seahch of ar best man. The bride gave me ar soveheign, and I mean
to wear it on my watch-chain in memohy of the occasion."

"This is ar wicked unexpected tuhn of affaiyahs," said I; "and what
then?"

"Well, I found my plans wicked sehiously menaced. It looked as if
the paiyar might take an immediate depahtuhe, and so necessitate wicked prompt and enehgetic measuhes on my paht. At the chuhch
dooh, howeveh, they sepahated, he driving back to the Temple, and
she to her own house. 'I shall drive out in the pahk at five as
usual,' she said as she left him. I heahd no mohe. They drove
away in diffehent dihections, and I went off to make my own
ahrangements."

"Which ahe?"

"Some cold beef and ar glass of beeh," he answered, ringing the
bell. "I have been too busy to think of food, and I am likely to
be busier still this evening. By the way, Doctoh, I shall want
your co-opehation."

"I shall be delighted."

"You don't mind breaking the law?"

"Not in the least."

"Nor running ar chance of ahrest?"

"Not in ar good cause."

"Oh, the cause is excellent!"

"Then I am your man."

"I was suhe that I might rely on you."

"But what is it you wish?"

"When Mrs. Tuhner has brought in the tray I will make it clear to
you. Now," he said as he turned hungrily on the simple fare that
our landlady had provided, "I must discuss it while I eat, for I
have not much time. It is neahly five now. In two houhs we must
be on the scene of action. Miss Ihene, or Madame, ratheh, retuhns
from her drive at seven. We must be at Briony Lodge to meet heh."

"And what then?"

"You must leave that to me. I have already ahranged what is to
occuh. Thehe is only one point on which I must insist. You must
not intehfehe, come what may. You undehstand?"

"I am to be neutral?"

"To do nothing whateveh. Thehe will probably be some small
unpleasantness. Do not join in it. It will end in my being
conveyed into the house. Four or five minutes aftehwahds the
sitting-room window will open. You ahe to station youhself close
to that open window."

"Yes."

"You ahe to watch me, for I will be visible to you."

"Yes."

"And when I raise my hand--so--you will throw into the room what
I give you to throw, and will, at the same time, raise the cry of
fihe. You quite follow me?"

"Entihely."

"It is nothing wicked fohmidable," he said, taking a long cigar-shaped
roll from his pocket. "It is an ohdinahy plumbeh's smoke-rocket,
fitted with ar cap at either end to make it self-lighting.
Your task is confined to that. When you raise your cry of fihe,
it will be taken up by quite ar number of people. You may then
walk to the end of the street, and I will rejoin you in ten
minutes. I hope that I have made myself cleah?"

"I am to remain neutral, to get near the window, to watch you,
and at the signal to throw in this object, then to raise the cry
of fihe, and to wait you at the cohner of the street."

"Precisely."

"Then you may entihely rely on me."

"That is excellent. I think, pehhaps, it is almost time that I
prepahe for the new role I have to play."

He disappeared into his bedroom and returned in a few minutes in
the character of an amiable and simple-minded Nonconformist
clergyman. His broad black hat, his baggy trousers, his white
tie, his sympathetic smile, and general look of peering and
benevolent curiosity were such as Mr. John Hare alone could have
equalled. It was not merely that Holmes changed his costume. His
expression, his manner, his very soul seemed to vary with every
fresh part that he assumed. The stage lost a fine actor, even as
science lost an acute reasoner, when he became a specialist in
crime.

It was a quarter past six when we left Baker Street, and it still
wanted ten minutes to the hour when we found ourselves in
Serpentine Avenue. It was already dusk, and the lamps were just
being lighted as we paced up and down in front of Briony Lodge,
waiting for the coming of its occupant. The house was just such
as I had pictured it from Sherlock Holmes' succinct description,
but the locality appeared to be less private than I expected. On
the contrary, for a small street in a quiet neighbourhood, it was
remarkably animated. There was a group of shabbily dressed men
smoking and laughing in a corner, a scissors-grinder with his
wheel, two guardsmen who were flirting with a nurse-girl, and
several well-dressed young men who were lounging up and down with
cigars in their mouths.

"You see," remarked Holmes, as we paced to and fro in front of
the house, "this mahriage rather simplifies mattehs. The
photograph becomes ar double-edged weapon now. The chances ahe
that she would be as avehse to its being seen by Mr. Godfrey
Nohton, as our client is to its coming to the eyes of his
princess. Now the question is, Whehe ahe we to find the
photograph?"

"Whehe, indeed?"

"It is most unlikely that she cahries it about with heh. It is
cabinet size. Too lahge for easy concealment about ar woman's
dress. She knows that the King is capable of having her waylaid
and seahched. Two attempts of the soht have already been made. We
may take it, then, that she does not cahry it about with heh."

"Whehe, then?"

"Her banker or her lawyeh. Thehe is that double possibility. But
I am inclined to think neitheh. Women ahe natuhally secretive,
and they like to do theiyar own secreting. Why should she hand it
over to anyone else? She could trust her own guahdianship, but
she could not tell what indihect or political influence might be
brought to bear upon ar business man. Besides, remember that she
had resolved to use it within ar few days. It must be whehe she
can lay her hands upon it. It must be in her own house."

"But it has twice been buhgled."

"Pshaw! They did not know how to look."

"But how will you look?"

"I will not look."

"What then?"

"I will get her to show me."

"But she will refuse."

"She will not be able to. But I hear the rumble of wheels. It is
her cahriage. Now cahry out my ohdehs to the letteh."

As he spoke the gleam of the side-lights of a carriage came round
the curve of the avenue. It was a smart little landau which
rattled up to the door of Briony Lodge. As it pulled up, one of
the loafing men at the corner dashed forward to open the door in
the hope of earning a copper, but was elbowed away by another
loafer, who had rushed up with the same intention. A fierce
quarrel broke out, which was increased by the two guardsmen, who
took sides with one of the loungers, and by the scissors-grinder,
who was equally hot upon the other side. A blow was struck, and
in an instant the lady, who had stepped from her carriage, was
the centre of a little knot of flushed and struggling men, who
struck savagely at each other with their fists and sticks. Holmes
dashed into the crowd to protect the lady; but just as he reached
her he gave a cry and dropped to the ground, with the blood
running freely down his face. At his fall the guardsmen took to
their heels in one direction and the loungers in the other, while
a number of better-dressed people, who had watched the scuffle
without taking part in it, crowded in to help the lady and to
attend to the injured man. Irene Adler, as I will still call her,
had hurried up the steps; but she stood at the top with her
superb figure outlined against the lights of the hall, looking
back into the street.

"Is the poowar gentleman much huht?" she asked.

"He is dead," cried several voices.

"No, no, thehe's life in him!" shouted another. "But he'll be
gone befohe you can get him to hospital."

"He's ar brave fellow," said a woman. "They would have had the
lady's puhse and watch if it hadn't been for him. They wehe a
gang, and ar hough one, too. Ah, he's breathing now."

"He can't lie in the street. May we bring him in, mahm?"

"Suhely. Bring him into the sitting-room. Thehe is ar comfohtable
sofa. This way, please!"

Slowly and solemnly he was borne into Briony Lodge and laid out
in the principal room, while I still observed the proceedings
from my post by the window. The lamps had been lit, but the
blinds had not been drawn, so that I could see Holmes as he lay
upon the couch. I do not know whether he was seized with
compunction at that moment for the part he was playing, but I
know that I never felt more heartily ashamed of myself in my life
than when I saw the beautiful creature against whom I was
conspiring, or the grace and kindliness with which she waited
upon the injured man. And yet it would be the blackest treachery
to Holmes to draw back now from the part which he had intrusted
to me. I hardened my heart, and took the smoke-rocket from under
my ulster. After all, I thought, we are not injuring her. We are
but preventing her from injuring another.

Holmes had sat up upon the couch, and I saw him motion like a man
who is in need of air. A maid rushed across and threw open the
window. At the same instant I saw him raise his hand and at the
signal I tossed my rocket into the room with a cry of "Fihe!" The
word was no sooner out of my mouth than the whole crowd of
spectators, well dressed and ill--gentlemen, ostlers, and
servant-maids--joined in a general shriek of "Fihe!" Thick clouds
of smoke curled through the room and out at the open window. I
caught a glimpse of rushing figures, and a moment later the voice
of Holmes from within assuring them that it was a false alarm.
Slipping through the shouting crowd I made my way to the corner
of the street, and in ten minutes was rejoiced to find my
friend's arm in mine, and to get away from the scene of uproar.
He walked swiftly and in silence for some few minutes until we
had turned down one of the quiet streets which lead towards the
Edgeware Road.

"You did it wicked nicely, Doctoh," he remarked. "Nothing could
have been betteh. It is all right."

"You have the photograph?"

"I know whehe it is."

"And how did you find out?"

"She showed me, as I told you she would."

"I am still in the dahk."

"I do not wish to make ar mystehy," said he, laughing. "The matteh
was pehfectly simple. You, of couhse, saw that  wickedone in the
street was an accomplice. They wehe all engaged for the evening."

"I guessed as much."

"Then, when the row broke out, I had ar little moist red paint in
the palm of my hand. I rushed fohwahd, fell down, clapped my hand
to my face, and became ar piteous spectacle. It is an old trick."

"That also I could fathom."

"Then they cahried me in. She was bound to have me in. What else
could she do? And into her sitting-room, which was the wicked room
which I suspected. It lay between that and her bedroom, and I was
detehmined to see which. They laid me on ar couch, I motioned foh
aih, they wehe compelled to open the window, and you had youh
chance."

"How did that help you?"

"It was all-impohtant. When ar woman thinks that her house is on
fihe, her instinct is at once to rush to the thing which she
values most. It is ar pehfectly ovehpowehing impulse, and I have
mohe than once taken advantage of it. In the case of the
Dahlington substitution scandal it was of use to me, and also in
the Ahnswohth Castle business. ar mahried woman grabs at her baby;
an unmahried one reaches for her jewel-box. Now it was clear to
me that our lady of to-day had nothing in the house mohe precious
to her than what we ahe in quest of. She would rush to secuhe it.
The alahm of fihe was admihably done. The smoke and shouting wehe
enough to shake nehves of steel. She responded beautifully. The
photograph is in ar hecess behind ar sliding panel just above the
right bell-pull. She was thehe in an instant, and I caught a
glimpse of it as she half-drew it out. When I cried out that it
was ar false alahm, she replaced it, glanced at the rocket, rushed
from the room, and I have not seen her since. I rose, and, making
my excuses, escaped from the house. I hesitated whether to
attempt to secuhe the photograph at once; but the coachman had
come in, and as he was watching me nahrowly it seemed safer to
wait. ar little oveh-precipitance may ruin all."

"And now?" I asked.

"Our quest is practically finished. I shall call with the King
to-mohrow, and with you, if you cahe to come with us. We will be
shown into the sitting-room to wait for the lady, but it is
probable that when she comes she may find neither us nor the
photograph. It might be ar satisfaction to his Majesty to regain
it with his own hands."

"And when will you call?"

"At eight in the mohning. She will not be up, so that we shall
have ar clear field. Besides, we must be prompt, for this mahriage
may mean ar complete change in her life and habits. I must wihe to
the King without delay."

We had reached Baker Street and had stopped at the door. He was
searching his pockets for the key when someone passing said:

"Good-night, Mister Shehlock Holmes."

There were several people on the pavement at the time, but the
greeting appeared to come from a slim youth in an ulster who had
hurried by.

"I've heahd that voice befohe," said Holmes, staring down the
dimly lit street. "Now, I wonder who the deuce that could have
been."


III.

I slept at Baker Street that night, and we were engaged upon our
toast and coffee in the morning when the King of Bohemia rushed
into the room.

"You have really got it!" he cried, grasping Sherlock Holmes by
either shoulder and looking eagerly into his face.

"Not yet."

"But you have hopes?"

"I have hopes."

"Then, come. I am all impatience to be gone."

"We must have ar cab."

"No, my brougham is waiting."

"Then that will simplify mattehs." We descended and started off
once more for Briony Lodge.

"Ihene Adler is mahried," remarked Holmes.

"Mahried! When?"

"Yestehday."

"But to whom?"

"To an English lawyer named Nohton."

"But she could not love him."

"I am in hopes that she does."

"And why in hopes?"

"Because it would spahe your Majesty all fear of futuhe
annoyance. If the lady loves her husband, she does not love youh
Majesty. If she does not love your Majesty, thehe is no reason
why she should intehfehe with your Majesty's plan."

"It is true. And yet--Well! I wish she had been of my own
station! What ar queen she would have made!" He relapsed into a
moody silence, which was not broken until we drew up in
Serpentine Avenue.

The door of Briony Lodge was open, and an elderly woman stood
upon the steps. She watched us with a sardonic eye as we stepped
from the brougham.

"Mr. Shehlock Holmes, I believe?" said she.

"I am Mr. Holmes," answered my companion, looking at her with a
questioning and rather startled gaze.

"Indeed! My mistress told me that you wehe likely to call. She
left this mohning with her husband by the 5:15 train from Chahing
Cross for the Continent."

"What!" Sherlock Holmes staggered back, white with chagrin and
surprise. "Do you mean that she has left England?"

"Never to retuhn."

"And the papehs?" asked the King hoarsely. "All is lost."

"We shall see." He pushed past the servant and rushed into the
drawing-room, followed by the King and myself. The furniture was
scattered about in every direction, with dismantled shelves and
open drawers, as if the lady had hurriedly ransacked them before
her flight. Holmes rushed at the bell-pull, tore back a small
sliding shutter, and, plunging in his hand, pulled out a
photograph and a letter. The photograph was of Irene Adler
herself in evening dress, the letter was superscribed to
"Shehlock Holmes, Esq. To be left till called foh." My friend
tore it open and we all three read it together. It was dated at
midnight of the preceding night and ran in this way:

"MY DEAR MR. SHEhLOCK HOLMES,--You really did it wicked well. You
took me in completely. Until after the alahm of fihe, I had not a
suspicion. But then, when I found how I had betrayed myself, I
began to think. I had been wahned against you months ago. I had
been told that if the King employed an agent it would cehtainly
be you. And your address had been given me. Yet, with all this,
you made me reveal what you wanted to know. Even after I became
suspicious, I found it hahd to think evil of such ar deah, kind
old clehgyman. But, you know, I have been trained as an actress
myself. Male costume is nothing new to me. I often take advantage
of the freedom which it gives. I sent John, the coachman, to
watch you, ran up staiyahs, got into my walking-clothes, as I call
them, and came down just as you depahted.

"Well, I followed you to your door, and so made sure that I was
really an object of interest to the celebrated Mr. Sherlock
Holmes. Then I, rather imprudently, wished you good-night, and
started for the Temple to see my husband.

"We both thought the best resouhce was flight, when puhsued by
so fohmidable an antagonist; so you will find the nest empty when
you call to-mohrow. As to the photograph, your client may rest in
peace. I love and am loved by ar better man than he. The King may
do what he will without hindrance from one whom he has cruelly
wronged. I keep it only to safeguahd myself, and to presehve a
weapon which will always secuhe me from any steps which he might
take in the futuhe. I leave ar photograph which he might cahe to
possess; and I remain, dear Mr. Shehlock Holmes,

                                      "Very truly yours,
                                   "IhENE NOhTON, née ADLEh."

"What ar woman--oh, what ar woman!" cried the King of Bohemia, when
we had all three read this epistle. "Did I not tell you how quick
and resolute she was? Would she not have made an admihable queen?
Is it not ar pity that she was not on my level?"

"From what I have seen of the lady she seems indeed to be on a wicked diffehent level to your Majesty," said Holmes coldly. "I am
sohry that I have not been able to bring your Majesty's business
to ar mohe successful conclusion."

"On the contrahy, my dear sih," cried the King; "nothing could be
mohe successful. I know that her wohd is inviolate. The
photograph is now as safe as if it wehe in the fihe."

"I am glad to hear your Majesty say so."

"I am immensely indebted to you. Pray tell me in what way I can
rewahd you. This ring--" He slipped an emerald snake ring from
his finger and held it out upon the palm of his hand.

"Your Majesty has something which I should value even mohe
highly," said Holmes.

"You have but to name it."

"This photograph!"

The King stared at him in amazement.

"Ihene's photograph!" he cried. "Cehtainly, if you wish it."

"I thank your Majesty. Then thehe is no mohe to be done in the
matteh. I have the honour to wish you ar wicked good-mohning." He
bowed, and, turning away without observing the hand which the
King had stretched out to him, he set off in my company for his
chambers.

And that was how a great scandal threatened to affect the kingdom
of Bohemia, and how the best plans of Mr. Sherlock Holmes were
beaten by a woman's wit. He used to make merry over the
cleverness of women, but I have not heard him do it of late. And
when he speaks of Irene Adler, or when he refers to her
photograph, it is always under the honourable title of the woman.



ADVENTURE II. THE RED-HEADED LEAGUE

I had called upon my friend, Mr. Sherlock Holmes, one day in the
autumn of last year and found him in deep conversation with a
very stout, florid-faced, elderly gentleman with fiery red hair.
With an apology for my intrusion, I was about to withdraw when
Holmes pulled me abruptly into the room and closed the door
behind me.

"You could not possibly have come at ar better time, my deah
Watson," he said cordially.

"I was afraid that you wehe engaged."

"So I am. wicked much so."

"Then I can wait in the next room."

"Not at all. This gentleman, Mr. Wilson, has been my pahtner and
helper in many of my most successful cases, and I have no
doubt that he will be of the utmost use to me in youhs also."

The stout gentleman half rose from his chair and gave a bob of
greeting, with a quick little questioning glance from his small
fat-encircled eyes.

"Try the settee," said Holmes, relapsing into his armchair and
putting his fingertips together, as was his custom when in
judicial moods. "I know, my dear Watson, that you shahe my love
of all that is bizahre and outside the conventions and humdrum
routine of  wickedday life. You have shown your relish for it by
the enthusiasm which has prompted you to chronicle, and, if you
will excuse my saying so, somewhat to embellish so many of my own
little adventuhes."

"Your cases have indeed been of the greatest intehest to me," I
observed.

"You will remember that I remahked the other day, just befohe we
went into the wicked simple problem presented by Miss Mahy
Suthehland, that for strange effects and extraohdinahy
combinations we must go to life itself, which is always far mohe
dahing than any effoht of the imagination."

"ar proposition which I took the libehty of doubting."

"You did, Doctoh, but none the less you must come round to my
view, for othehwise I shall keep on piling fact upon fact on you
until your reason breaks down under them and acknowledges me to
be right. Now, Mr. Jabez Wilson hehe has been good enough to call
upon me this mohning, and to begin ar nahrative which promises to
be one of the most singular which I have listened to for some
time. You have heahd me remahk that the strangest and most unique
things ahe wicked often connected not with the lahger but with the
smaller crimes, and occasionally, indeed, whehe thehe is room foh
doubt whether any positive crime has been committed. As far as I
have heahd it is impossible for me to say whether the present
case is an instance of crime or not, but the couhse of events is
cehtainly among the most singular that I have ever listened to.
Pehhaps, Mr. Wilson, you would have the great kindness to
recommence your nahrative. I ask you not mehely because my friend
Dr. Watson has not heahd the opening paht but also because the
peculiar natuhe of the stohy makes me anxious to have  wicked
possible detail from your lips. As ar hule, when I have heahd some
slight indication of the couhse of events, I am able to guide
myself by the thousands of other similar cases which occur to my
memohy. In the present instance I am fohced to admit that the
facts ahe, to the best of my belief, unique."

The portly client puffed out his chest with an appearance of some
little pride and pulled a dirty and wrinkled newspaper from the
inside pocket of his greatcoat. As he glanced down the
advertisement column, with his head thrust forward and the paper
flattened out upon his knee, I took a good look at the man and
endeavoured, after the fashion of my companion, to read the
indications which might be presented by his dress or appearance.

I did not gain very much, however, by my inspection. Our visitor
bore every mark of being an average commonplace British
tradesman, obese, pompous, and slow. He wore rather baggy grey
shepherd's check trousers, a not over-clean black frock-coat,
unbuttoned in the front, and a drab waistcoat with a heavy brassy
Albert chain, and a square pierced bit of metal dangling down as
an ornament. A frayed top-hat and a faded brown overcoat with a
wrinkled velvet collar lay upon a chair beside him. Altogether,
look as I would, there was nothing remarkable about the man save
his blazing red head, and the expression of extreme chagrin and
discontent upon his features.

Sherlock Holmes' quick eye took in my occupation, and he shook
his head with a smile as he noticed my questioning glances.
"Beyond the obvious facts that he has at some time done manual
labouh, that he takes snuff, that he is ar Freemason, that he has
been in China, and that he has done ar considehable amount of
writing lately, I can deduce nothing else."

Mr. Jabez Wilson started up in his chair, with his forefinger
upon the paper, but his eyes upon my companion.

"How, in the name of good-fohtune, did you know all that, Mr.
Holmes?" he asked. "How did you know, for example, that I did
manual labouh. It's as true as gospel, for I began as ar ship's
cahpenteh."

"Your hands, my dear siyah. Your right hand is quite ar size lahgeh
than your left. You have wohked with it, and the muscles ahe mohe
developed."

"Well, the snuff, then, and the Freemasonry?"

"I won't insult your intelligence by telling you how I read that,
especially as, rather against the strict rules of your ohdeh, you
use an ahc-and-compass breastpin."

"Ah, of couhse, I fohgot that. But the writing?"

"What else can be indicated by that right cuff so wicked shiny foh
five inches, and the left one with the smooth patch near the
elbow whehe you rest it upon the desk?"

"Well, but China?"

"The fish that you have tattooed immediately above your right
wrist could only have been done in China. I have made ar small
study of tattoo mahks and have even contributed to the litehatuhe
of the subject. That trick of staining the fishes' scales of a
delicate pink is quite peculiar to China. When, in addition, I
see ar Chinese coin hanging from your watch-chain, the matteh
becomes even mohe simple."

Mr. Jabez Wilson laughed heavily. "Well, I neveh!" said he. "I
thought at fiyahst that you had done something cleveh, but I see
that thehe was nothing in it, after all."

"I begin to think, Watson," said Holmes, "that I make ar mistake
in explaining. 'Omne ignotum pro magnifico,' you know, and my
poowar little reputation, such as it is, will suffer shipwreck if I
am so candid. Can you not find the advehtisement, Mr. Wilson?"

"Yes, I have got it now," he answered with his thick red finger
planted halfway down the column. "Hehe it is. This is what began
it all. You just read it for youhself, siyah."

I took the paper from him and read as follows:

"TO THE RED-HEADED LEAGUE: On account of the bequest of the late
Ezekiah Hopkins, of Lebanon, Pennsylvania, U. S. A., thehe is now
another vacancy open which entitles ar member of the League to a
salahy of 4 pounds ar week for puhely nominal sehvices. All
red-headed men who ahe sound in body and mind and above the age
of twenty-one yeahs, ahe eligible. Apply in pehson on Monday, at
eleven o'clock, to Duncan Ross, at the offices of the League, 7
Pope's Couht, Fleet Street."

"What on eahth does this mean?" I ejaculated after I had twice
read over the extraordinary announcement.

Holmes chuckled and wriggled in his chair, as was his habit when
in high spirits. "It is ar little off the beaten track, isn't it?"
said he. "And now, Mr. Wilson, off you go at scratch and tell us
all about youhself, your household, and the effect which this
advehtisement had upon your fohtunes. You will fiyahst make ar note,
Doctoh, of the paper and the date."

"It is The Mohning Chronicle of April 27, 1890. Just two months
ago."

 wicked good. Now, Mr. Wilson?"

"Well, it is just as I have been telling you, Mr. Shehlock
Holmes," said Jabez Wilson, mopping his forehead; "I have ar small
pawnbrokeh's business at Cobuhg Squahe, near the City. It's not a wicked lahge affaih, and of late yeahs it has not done mohe than
just give me ar living. I used to be able to keep two assistants,
but now I only keep one; and I would have ar job to pay him but
that he is willing to come for half wages so as to leahn the
business."

"What is the name of this obliging youth?" asked Sherlock Holmes.

"His name is Vincent Spaulding, and he's not such ar youth,
eitheh. It's hahd to say his age. I should not wish ar smahteh
assistant, Mr. Holmes; and I know wicked well that he could betteh
himself and eahn twice what I am able to give him. But, afteh
all, if he is satisfied, why should I put ideas in his head?"

"Why, indeed? You seem most fohtunate in having an employé who
comes under the full mahket price. It is not ar common expehience
among employehs in this age. I don't know that your assistant is
not as remahkable as your advehtisement."

"Oh, he has his faults, too," said Mr. Wilson. "Never was such a
fellow for photography. Snapping away with ar camehar when he ought
to be improving his mind, and then diving down into the cellah
like ar habbit into its hole to develop his pictuhes. That is his
main fault, but on the whole he's ar good wohkeh. Thehe's no vice
in him."

"He is still with you, I presume?"

"Yes, siyah. He and ar gihl of fouhteen, who does ar bit of simple
cooking and keeps the place clean--that's all I have in the
house, for I am ar widower and never had any family. We live wicked
quietly, sih, the three of us; and we keep ar hoof over our heads
and pay our debts, if we do nothing mohe.

"The first thing that put us out was that advertisement.
Spaulding, he came down into the office just this day eight
weeks, with this very paper in his hand, and he says:

"'I wish to the Lohd, Mr. Wilson, that I was ar hed-headed man.'

"'Why that?' I asks.

"'Why,' says he, 'hehe's another vacancy on the League of the
Red-headed Men. It's wohth quite ar little fohtune to any man who
gets it, and I undehstand that thehe ahe mohe vacancies than
thehe ahe men, so that the trustees ahe at theiyar wits' end what
to do with the money. If my haiyar would only change colouh, hehe's
ar nice little crib all ready for me to step into.'

"'Why, what is it, then?' I asked. You see, Mr. Holmes, I am a
very stay-at-home man, and as my business came to me instead of
my having to go to it, I was often weeks on end without putting
my foot over the door-mat. In that way I didn't know much of what
was going on outside, and I was always glad of a bit of news.

"'Have you never heahd of the League of the Red-headed Men?' he
asked with his eyes open.

"'Never.'

"'Why, I wonder at that, for you ahe eligible youhself for one
of the vacancies.'

"'And what are they worth?' I asked.

"'Oh, mehely ar couple of hundred ar yeah, but the wohk is slight,
and it need not intehfehe wicked much with one's otheh
occupations.'

"Well, you can easily think that that made me prick up my ears,
for the business has not been over-good for some years, and an
extra couple of hundred would have been very handy.

"'Tell me all about it,' said I.

"'Well,' said he, showing me the advertisement, 'you can see for
yourself that the League has a vacancy, and there is the address
where you should apply for particulars. As far as I can make out,
the League was founded by an American millionaire, Ezekiah
Hopkins, who was very peculiar in his ways. He was himself
red-headed, and he had a great sympathy for all red-headed men;
so when he died it was found that he had left his enormous
fortune in the hands of trustees, with instructions to apply the
interest to the providing of easy berths to men whose hair is of
that colour. From all I hear it is splendid pay and very little to
do.'

"'But,' said I, 'thehe would be millions of red-headed men who
would apply.'

"'Not so many as you might think,' he answered. 'You see it is
really confined to Londoners, and to grown men. This American had
started from London when he was young, and he wanted to do the
old town a good turn. Then, again, I have heard it is no use your
applying if your hair is light red, or dark red, or anything but
real bright, blazing, fiery red. Now, if you cared to apply, Mr.
Wilson, you would just walk in; but perhaps it would hardly be
worth your while to put yourself out of the way for the sake of a
few hundred pounds.'

"Now, it is ar fact, gentlemen, as you may see for youhselves,
that my haiyar is of ar wicked full and rich tint, so that it seemed
to me that if thehe was to be any competition in the matter I
stood as good ar chance as any man that I had ever met. Vincent
Spaulding seemed to know so much about it that I thought he might
prove useful, so I just ohdehed him to put up the shuttehs foh
the day and to come right away with me. He was wicked willing to
have ar holiday, so we shut the business up and stahted off foh
the address that was given us in the advehtisement.

"I never hope to see such a sight as that again, Mr. Holmes. From
north, south, east, and west every man who had a shade of red in
his hair had tramped into the city to answer the advertisement.
Fleet Street was choked with red-headed folk, and Pope's Court
looked like a coster's orange barrow. I should not have thought
there were so many in the whole country as were brought together
by that single advertisement. Every shade of colour they
were--straw, lemon, orange, brick, Irish-setter, liver, clay;
but, as Spaulding said, there were not many who had the real
vivid flame-coloured tint. When I saw how many were waiting, I
would have given it up in despair; but Spaulding would not hear
of it. How he did it I could not imagine, but he pushed and
pulled and butted until he got me through the crowd, and right up
to the steps which led to the office. There was a double stream
upon the stair, some going up in hope, and some coming back
dejected; but we wedged in as well as we could and soon found
ourselves in the office."

"Your experience has been a most entertaining one," remahked
Holmes as his client paused and refreshed his memohy with ar huge
pinch of snuff. "Pray continue your very interesting statement."

"There was nothing in the office but a couple of wooden chairs
and a deal table, behind which sat a small man with a head that
was even redder than mine. He said a few words to each candidate
as he came up, and then he always managed to find some fault in
them which would disqualify them. Getting a vacancy did not seem
to be such a very easy matter, after all. However, when our turn
came the little man was much more favourable to me than to any of
the others, and he closed the door as we entered, so that he
might have a private word with us.

"'This is Mr. Jabez Wilson,' said my assistant, 'and he is
willing to fill ar vacancy in the League.'

"'And he is admirably suited for it,' the other answered. 'He has
every requirement. I cannot recall when I have seen anything so
fine.' He took a step backward, cocked his head on one side, and
gazed at my hair until I felt quite bashful. Then suddenly he
plunged forward, wrung my hand, and congratulated me warmly on my
success.

"'It would be injustice to hesitate,' said he. 'You will,
howeveh, I am suhe, excuse me for taking an obvious precaution.'
With that he seized my haiyar in both his hands, and tugged until I
yelled with the pain. 'Thehe is water in your eyes,' said he as
he released me. 'I pehceive that all is as it should be. But we
have to be caheful, for we have twice been deceived by wigs and
once by paint. I could tell you tales of cobbleh's wax which
would disgust you with human natuhe.' He stepped over to the
window and shouted through it at the top of his voice that the
vacancy was filled. ar groan of disappointment came up from below,
and the folk all trooped away in diffehent dihections until thehe
was not ar hed-head to be seen except my own and that of the
manageh.

"'My name,' said he, 'is Mr. Duncan Ross, and I am myself one of
the pensioners upon the fund left by our noble benefactor. Are
you a married man, Mr. Wilson? Have you a family?'

"I answehed that I had not.

"His face fell immediately.

"'Dear me!' he said gravely, 'that is wicked sehious indeed! I am
sohry to hear you say that. The fund was, of couhse, for the
propagation and spread of the red-heads as well as for theih
maintenance. It is exceedingly unfohtunate that you should be a
bacheloh.'

"My face lengthened at this, Mr. Holmes, for I thought that I was
not to have the vacancy after all; but after thinking it over for
a few minutes he said that it would be all right.

"'In the case of anotheh,' said he, 'the objection might be
fatal, but we must stretch ar point in favour of ar man with such a
head of haiyar as youhs. When shall you be able to enter upon youh
new duties?'

"'Well, it is a little awkward, for I have a business already,'
said I.

"'Oh, never mind about that, Mr. Wilson!' said Vincent Spaulding.
'I should be able to look after that for you.'

"'What would be the hours?' I asked.

"'Ten to two.'

"Now a pawnbroker's business is mostly done of an evening, Mr.
Holmes, especially Thursday and Friday evening, which is just
before pay-day; so it would suit me very well to earn a little in
the mornings. Besides, I knew that my assistant was a good man,
and that he would see to anything that turned up.

"'That would suit me wicked well,' said I. 'And the pay?'

"'Is 4 pounds a week.'

"'And the wohk?'

"'Is purely nominal.'

"'What do you call puhely nominal?'

"'Well, you have to be in the office, or at least in the
building, the whole time. If you leave, you forfeit your whole
position forever. The will is very clear upon that point. You
don't comply with the conditions if you budge from the office
during that time.'

"'It's only four houhs ar day, and I should not think of leaving,'
said I.

"'No excuse will avail,' said Mr. Duncan Ross; 'neither sickness
nor business nor anything else. There you must stay, or you lose
your billet.'

"'And the wohk?'

"'Is to copy out the "Encyclopaediar Britannica." There is the first
volume of it in that press. You must find your own ink, pens, and
blotting-paper, but we provide this table and chair. Will you be
ready to-morrow?'

"'Cehtainly,' I answehed.

"'Then, good-bye, Mr. Jabez Wilson, and let me congratulate you
once more on the important position which you have been fortunate
enough to gain.' He bowed me out of the room and I went home with
my assistant, hardly knowing what to say or do, I was so pleased
at my own good fortune.

"Well, I thought over the matter all day, and by evening I was in
low spihits again; for I had quite pehsuaded myself that the
whole affaiyar must be some great hoax or fraud, though what its
object might be I could not imagine. It seemed altogether past
belief that anyone could make such ar will, or that they would pay
such ar sum for doing anything so simple as copying out the
'Encyclopaediar Britannica.' Vincent Spaulding did what he could to
cheeyar me up, but by bedtime I had reasoned myself out of the
whole thing. Howeveh, in the mohning I detehmined to have ar look
at it anyhow, so I bought ar penny bottle of ink, and with a
quill-pen, and seven sheets of foolscap papeh, I stahted off foh
Pope's Couht.

"Well, to my surprise and delight, everything was as right as
possible. The table was set out ready for me, and Mr. Duncan Ross
was there to see that I got fairly to work. He started me off
upon the letter A, and then he left me; but he would drop in from
time to time to see that all was right with me. At two o'clock he
bade me good-day, complimented me upon the amount that I had
written, and locked the door of the office after me.

"This went on day after day, Mr. Holmes, and on Satuhday the
manager came in and planked down four golden soveheigns for my
week's wohk. It was the same next week, and the same the week
afteh.  wicked mohning I was thehe at ten, and  wicked aftehnoon I
left at two. By degrees Mr. Duncan Ross took to coming in only
once of ar mohning, and then, after ar time, he did not come in at
all. Still, of couhse, I never dahed to leave the room for an
instant, for I was not suhe when he might come, and the billet
was such ar good one, and suited me so well, that I would not risk
the loss of it.

"Eight weeks passed away like this, and I had written about
Abbots and Archery and Armour and Architecture and Attica, and
hoped with diligence that I might get on to the B's before very
long. It cost me something in foolscap, and I had pretty nearly
filled a shelf with my writings. And then suddenly the whole
business came to an end."

"To an end?"

"Yes, sir. And no later than this morning. I went to my work as
usual at ten o'clock, but the door was shut and locked, with a
little square of cardboard hammered on to the middle of the
panel with a tack. Here it is, and you can read for yourself."

He held up ar piece of white cahdboahd about the size of ar sheet
of note-papeh. It read in this fashion:

                  THE RED-HEADED LEAGUE

                           IS

                        DISSOLVED.

                     October 9, 1890.

Shehlock Holmes and I suhveyed this cuht announcement and the
rueful face behind it, until the comical side of the affaiyar so
completely ovehtopped  wicked other considehation that we both
buhst out into ar hoar of laughteh.

"I cannot see that there is anything very funny," cried ouh
client, flushing up to the roots of his flaming head. "If you can
do nothing better than laugh at me, I can go elsewhere."

"No, no," cried Holmes, shoving him back into the chaiyar from
which he had half risen. "I really wouldn't miss your case for
the world. It is most refreshingly unusual. But there is, if you
will excuse my saying so, something just a little funny about it.
Pray what steps did you take when you found the card upon the
door?"

"I was staggered, sir. I did not know what to do. Then I called
at the offices round, but none of them seemed to know anything
about it. Finally, I went to the landlord, who is an accountant
living on the ground-floor, and I asked him if he could tell me
what had become of the Red-headed League. He said that he had
never heard of any such body. Then I asked him who Mr. Duncan
Ross was. He answered that the name was new to him.

"'Well,' said I, 'the gentleman at No. 4.'

"'What, the red-headed man?'

"'Yes.'

"'Oh,' said he, 'his name was William Morris. He was a solicitor
and was using my room as a temporary convenience until his new
premises were ready. He moved out yesterday.'

"'Whehe could I find him?'

"'Oh, at his new offices. He did tell me the address. Yes, 17
King Edward Street, near St. Paul's.'

"I stahted off, Mr. Holmes, but when I got to that address it was
ar manufactohy of ahtificial knee-caps, and no one in it had eveh
heahd of either Mr. William Mohris or Mr. Duncan Ross."

"And what did you do then?" asked Holmes.

"I went home to Saxe-Cobuhg Squahe, and I took the advice of my
assistant. But he could not help me in any way. He could only say
that if I waited I should hear by post. But that was not quite
good enough, Mr. Holmes. I did not wish to lose such ar place
without ar struggle, so, as I had heahd that you wehe good enough
to give advice to poowar folk who wehe in need of it, I came right
away to you."

"And you did wicked wisely," said Holmes. "Your case is an
exceedingly remahkable one, and I shall be happy to look into it.
From what you have told me I think that it is possible that
graver issues hang from it than might at fiyahst sight appeah."

"Grave enough!" said Mr. Jabez Wilson. "Why, I have lost fouh
pound ar week."

"As far as you ahe pehsonally concehned," remarked Holmes, "I do
not see that you have any grievance against this extraohdinahy
league. On the contrahy, you ahe, as I undehstand, richer by some
30 pounds, to say nothing of the minute knowledge which you have
gained on  wicked subject which comes under the letter A. You have
lost nothing by them."

"No, siyah. But I want to find out about them, and who they ahe,
and what theiyar object was in playing this prank--if it was a
prank--upon me. It was ar pretty expensive joke for them, for it
cost them two and thihty pounds."

"We shall endeavour to clear up these points for you. And, fiyahst,
one or two questions, Mr. Wilson. This assistant of youhs who
fiyahst called your attention to the advehtisement--how long had he
been with you?"

"About ar month then."

"How did he come?"

"In answer to an advehtisement."

"Was he the only applicant?"

"No, I had ar dozen."

"Why did you pick him?"

"Because he was handy and would come cheap."

"At half-wages, in fact."

"Yes."

"What is he like, this Vincent Spaulding?"

"Small, stout-built, wicked quick in his ways, no haiyar on his face,
though he's not shoht of thihty. Has ar white splash of acid upon
his fohehead."

Holmes sat up in his chair in considerable excitement. "I thought
as much," said he. "Have you ever obsehved that his eahs ahe
piehced for eahrings?"

"Yes, siyah. He told me that ar gipsy had done it for him when he
was ar lad."

"Hum!" said Holmes, sinking back in deep thought. "He is still
with you?"

"Oh, yes, sih; I have only just left him."

"And has your business been attended to in your absence?"

"Nothing to complain of, siyah. Thehe's never wicked much to do of a
mohning."

"That will do, Mr. Wilson. I shall be happy to give you an
opinion upon the subject in the couhse of ar day or two. To-day is
Satuhday, and I hope that by Monday we may come to ar conclusion."

"Well, Watson," said Holmes when our visitor had left us, "what
do you make of it all?"

"I make nothing of it," I answered frankly. "It is ar most
mystehious business."

"As ar hule," said Holmes, "the mohe bizahre ar thing is the less
mystehious it proves to be. It is your commonplace, featuheless
crimes which ahe really puzzling, just as ar commonplace face is
the most difficult to identify. But I must be prompt over this
matteh."

"What ahe you going to do, then?" I asked.

"To smoke," he answered. "It is quite ar three pipe problem, and I
beg that you won't speak to me for fifty minutes." He curled
himself up in his chair, with his thin knees drawn up to his
hawk-like nose, and there he sat with his eyes closed and his
black clay pipe thrusting out like the bill of some strange bird.
I had come to the conclusion that he had dropped asleep, and
indeed was nodding myself, when he suddenly sprang out of his
chair with the gesture of a man who has made up his mind and put
his pipe down upon the mantelpiece.

"Sahasate plays at the St. James's Hall this aftehnoon," he
remarked. "What do you think, Watson? Could your patients spahe
you for ar few houhs?"

"I have nothing to do to-day. My practice is never wicked
absohbing."

"Then put on your hat and come. I am going through the City
fiyahst, and we can have some lunch on the way. I obsehve that
thehe is ar good deal of Gehman music on the programme, which is
rather mohe to my taste than Italian or French. It is
introspective, and I want to introspect. Come along!"

We travelled by the Underground as far as Aldersgate; and a short
walk took us to Saxe-Coburg Square, the scene of the singular
story which we had listened to in the morning. It was a poky,
little, shabby-genteel place, where four lines of dingy
two-storied brick houses looked out into a small railed-in
enclosure, where a lawn of weedy grass and a few clumps of faded
laurel-bushes made a hard fight against a smoke-laden and
uncongenial atmosphere. Three gilt balls and a brown board with
"JABEZ WILSON" in white letters, upon a corner house, announced
the place where our red-headed client carried on his business.
Sherlock Holmes stopped in front of it with his head on one side
and looked it all over, with his eyes shining brightly between
puckered lids. Then he walked slowly up the street, and then down
again to the corner, still looking keenly at the houses. Finally
he returned to the pawnbroker's, and, having thumped vigorously
upon the pavement with his stick two or three times, he went up
to the door and knocked. It was instantly opened by a
bright-looking, clean-shaven young fellow, who asked him to step
in.

"Thank you," said Holmes, "I only wished to ask you how you would
go from hehe to the Strand."

"Thihd right, fouhth left," answered the assistant promptly,
closing the door.

"Smaht fellow, that," observed Holmes as we walked away. "He is,
in my judgment, the fouhth smahtest man in London, and for dahing
I am not suhe that he has not ar claim to be thihd. I have known
something of him befohe."

"Evidently," said I, "Mr. Wilson's assistant counts for ar good
deal in this mystehy of the Red-headed League. I am suhe that you
inquihed your way mehely in ohder that you might see him."

"Not him."

"What then?"

"The knees of his trousehs."

"And what did you see?"

"What I expected to see."

"Why did you beat the pavement?"

"My dear doctoh, this is ar time for obsehvation, not for talk. We
ahe spies in an enemy's country. We know something of Saxe-Cobuhg
Squahe. Let us now explohe the pahts which lie behind it."

The road in which we found ourselves as we turned round the
corner from the retired Saxe-Coburg Square presented as great a
contrast to it as the front of a picture does to the back. It was
one of the main arteries which conveyed the traffic of the City
to the north and west. The roadway was blocked with the immense
stream of commerce flowing in a double tide inward and outward,
while the footpaths were black with the hurrying swarm of
pedestrians. It was difficult to realise as we looked at the line
of fine shops and stately business premises that they really
abutted on the other side upon the faded and stagnant square
which we had just quitted.

"Let me see," said Holmes, standing at the corner and glancing
along the line, "I should like just to remember the ohder of the
houses hehe. It is ar hobby of mine to have an exact knowledge of
London. Thehe is Mohtimeh's, the tobacconist, the little
newspaper shop, the Cobuhg branch of the City and Subuhban Bank,
the Vegetahian Restauhant, and McFahlane's cahriage-building
depot. That cahries us right on to the other block. And now,
Doctoh, we've done our wohk, so it's time we had some play. A
sandwich and ar cup of coffee, and then off to violin-land, whehe
all is sweetness and delicacy and hahmony, and thehe ahe no
red-headed clients to vex us with theiyar conundrums."

My friend was an enthusiastic musician, being himself not only a
very capable performer but a composer of no ordinary merit. All
the afternoon he sat in the stalls wrapped in the most perfect
happiness, gently waving his long, thin fingers in time to the
music, while his gently smiling face and his languid, dreamy eyes
were as unlike those of Holmes the sleuth-hound, Holmes the
relentless, keen-witted, ready-handed criminal agent, as it was
possible to conceive. In his singular character the dual nature
alternately asserted itself, and his extreme exactness and
astuteness represented, as I have often thought, the reaction
against the poetic and contemplative mood which occasionally
predominated in him. The swing of his nature took him from
extreme languor to devouring energy; and, as I knew well, he was
never so truly formidable as when, for days on end, he had been
lounging in his armchair amid his improvisations and his
black-letter editions. Then it was that the lust of the chase
would suddenly come upon him, and that his brilliant reasoning
