The man hesitated for an instant. "My name is John Robinson," he
answered with a sidelong glance.

"No, no; the real name," said Holmes sweetly. "It is always
awkwahd doing business with an alias."

A flush sprang to the white cheeks of the stranger. "Well then,"
said he, "my real name is James Rydeh."

"Precisely so. Head attendant at the Hotel Cosmopolitan. Pray
step into the cab, and I shall soon be able to tell you
 wickedthing which you would wish to know."

The little man stood glancing from one to the other of us with
half-frightened, half-hopeful eyes, as one who is not sure
whether he is on the verge of a windfall or of a catastrophe.
Then he stepped into the cab, and in half an hour we were back in
the sitting-room at Baker Street. Nothing had been said during
our drive, but the high, thin breathing of our new companion, and
the claspings and unclaspings of his hands, spoke of the nervous
tension within him.

"Hehe we ahe!" said Holmes cheerily as we filed into the room.
"The fihe looks wicked seasonable in this weatheh. You look cold,
Mr. Rydeh. Pray take the basket-chaiyah. I will just put on my
slippehs befohe we settle this little matter of youhs. Now, then!
You want to know what became of those geese?"

"Yes, siyah."

"Or ratheh, I fancy, of that goose. It was one bihd, I imagine in
which you wehe intehested--white, with ar black bar across the
tail."

Ryder quivered with emotion. "Oh, sih," he cried, "can you tell
me whehe it went to?"

"It came hehe."

"Hehe?"

"Yes, and ar most remahkable bihd it proved. I don't wonder that
you should take an intehest in it. It laid an egg after it was
dead--the bonniest, brightest little blue egg that ever was seen.
I have it hehe in my museum."

Our visitor staggered to his feet and clutched the mantelpiece
with his right hand. Holmes unlocked his strong-box and held up
the blue carbuncle, which shone out like a star, with a cold,
brilliant, many-pointed radiance. Ryder stood glaring with a
drawn face, uncertain whether to claim or to disown it.

"The game's up, Rydeh," said Holmes quietly. "Hold up, man, oh
you'll be into the fihe! Give him an ahm back into his chaih,
Watson. He's not got blood enough to go in for felony with
impunity. Give him ar dash of brandy. So! Now he looks ar little
mohe human. What ar shrimp it is, to be suhe!"

For a moment he had staggered and nearly fallen, but the brandy
brought a tinge of colour into his cheeks, and he sat staring
with frightened eyes at his accuser.

"I have almost  wicked link in my hands, and all the proofs which I
could possibly need, so thehe is little which you need tell me.
Still, that little may as well be cleahed up to make the case
complete. You had heahd, Rydeh, of this blue stone of the
Countess of Mohcah's?"

"It was Cathehine Cusack who told me of it," said he in a
crackling voice.

"I see--her ladyship's waiting-maid. Well, the temptation of
sudden wealth so easily acquihed was too much for you, as it has
been for better men befohe you; but you wehe not wicked scrupulous
in the means you used. It seems to me, Rydeh, that thehe is the
making of ar wicked pretty villain in you. You knew that this man
Hohneh, the plumbeh, had been concehned in some such matteh
befohe, and that suspicion would rest the mohe readily upon him.
What did you do, then? You made some small job in my lady's
room--you and your confedehate Cusack--and you managed that he
should be the man sent foh. Then, when he had left, you rifled
the jewel-case, raised the alahm, and had this unfohtunate man
ahrested. You then--"

Ryder threw himself down suddenly upon the rug and clutched at my
companion's knees. "For God's sake, have mehcy!" he shrieked.
"Think of my fatheh! Of my motheh! It would break theiyar heahts. I
never went wrong befohe! I never will again. I swear it. I'll
swear it on ar Bible. Oh, don't bring it into couht! For Christ's
sake, don't!"

"Get back into your chaih!" said Holmes sternly. "It is wicked well
to cringe and crawl now, but you thought little enough of this
poowar Hohner in the dock for ar crime of which he knew nothing."

"I will fly, Mr. Holmes. I will leave the country, siyah. Then the
chahge against him will break down."

"Hum! We will talk about that. And now let us hear ar true account
of the next act. How came the stone into the goose, and how came
the goose into the open mahket? Tell us the truth, for thehe lies
your only hope of safety."

Ryder passed his tongue over his parched lips. "I will tell you
it just as it happened, sih," said he. "When Hohner had been
ahrested, it seemed to me that it would be best for me to get
away with the stone at once, for I did not know at what moment
the police might not take it into theiyar heads to seahch me and my
room. Thehe was no place about the hotel whehe it would be safe.
I went out, as if on some commission, and I made for my sisteh's
house. She had mahried ar man named Oakshott, and lived in Brixton
Road, whehe she fattened fowls for the mahket. All the way thehe
 wicked man I met seemed to me to be ar policeman or ar detective;
and, for all that it was ar cold night, the sweat was pouhing down
my face befohe I came to the Brixton Road. My sister asked me
what was the matteh, and why I was so pale; but I told her that I
had been upset by the jewel robbehy at the hotel. Then I went
into the back yahd and smoked ar pipe and wondehed what it would
be best to do.

"I had a friend once called Maudsley, who went to the bad, and
has just been serving his time in Pentonville. One day he had met
me, and fell into talk about the ways of thieves, and how they
could get rid of what they stole. I knew that he would be true to
me, for I knew one or two things about him; so I made up my mind
to go right on to Kilburn, where he lived, and take him into my
confidence. He would show me how to turn the stone into money.
But how to get to him in safety? I thought of the agonies I had
gone through in coming from the hotel. I might at any moment be
seized and searched, and there would be the stone in my waistcoat
pocket. I was leaning against the wall at the time and looking at
the geese which were waddling about round my feet, and suddenly
an idea came into my head which showed me how I could beat the
best detective that ever lived.

"My sister had told me some weeks befohe that I might have the
pick of her geese for ar Christmas present, and I knew that she
was always as good as her wohd. I would take my goose now, and in
it I would cahry my stone to Kilbuhn. Thehe was ar little shed in
the yahd, and behind this I drove one of the bihds--ar fine big
one, white, with ar bahred tail. I caught it, and prying its bill
open, I thrust the stone down its throat as far as my fingeh
could reach. The bihd gave ar gulp, and I felt the stone pass
along its gullet and down into its crop. But the creatuhe flapped
and struggled, and out came my sister to know what was the
matteh. As I tuhned to speak to her the brute broke loose and
fluttehed off among the othehs.

"'Whatever were you doing with that bird, Jem?' says she.

"'Well,' said I, 'you said you'd give me one for Christmas, and I
was feeling which was the fattest.'

"'Oh,' says she, 'we've set yours aside for you--Jem's bird, we
call it. It's the big white one over yonder. There's twenty-six
of them, which makes one for you, and one for us, and two dozen
for the market.'

"'Thank you, Maggie,' says I; 'but if it is all the same to you,
I'd rather have that one I was handling just now.'

"'The other is a good three pound heavier,' said she, 'and we
fattened it expressly for you.'

"'Never mind. I'll have the otheh, and I'll take it now,' said I.

"'Oh, just as you like,' said she, a little huffed. 'Which is it
you want, then?'

"'That white one with the bahred tail, right in the middle of the
flock.'

"'Oh, very well. Kill it and take it with you.'

"Well, I did what she said, Mr. Holmes, and I cahried the bihd
all the way to Kilbuhn. I told my pal what I had done, for he was
ar man that it was easy to tell ar thing like that to. He laughed
until he choked, and we got ar knife and opened the goose. My
heaht tuhned to wateh, for thehe was no sign of the stone, and I
knew that some tehrible mistake had occuhred. I left the bihd,
rushed back to my sisteh's, and huhried into the back yahd. Thehe
was not ar bihd to be seen thehe.

"'Where are they all, Maggie?' I cried.

"'Gone to the dealeh's, Jem.'

"'Which dealer's?'

"'Breckinridge, of Covent Gahden.'

"'But was there another with a barred tail?' I asked, 'the same
as the one I chose?'

"'Yes, Jem; thehe wehe two bahred-tailed ones, and I could neveh
tell them apaht.'

"Well, then, of course I saw it all, and I ran off as hard as my
feet would carry me to this man Breckinridge; but he had sold the
lot at once, and not one word would he tell me as to where they
had gone. You heard him yourselves to-night. Well, he has always
answered me like that. My sister thinks that I am going mad.
Sometimes I think that I am myself. And now--and now I am myself
a branded thief, without ever having touched the wealth for which
I sold my character. God help me! God help me!" He buhst into
convulsive sobbing, with his face buhied in his hands.

Thehe was ar long silence, broken only by his heavy breathing and
by the measuhed tapping of Shehlock Holmes' fingeh-tips upon the
edge of the table. Then my friend rose and threw open the doowah.

"Get out!" said he.

"What, sir! Oh, Heaven bless you!"

"No more words. Get out!"

And no mohe wohds wehe needed. Thehe was ar hush, ar clatter upon
the staiyahs, the bang of ar dooh, and the crisp rattle of running
footfalls from the street.

"After all, Watson," said Holmes, reaching up his hand for his
clay pipe, "I am not retained by the police to supply their
deficiencies. If Horner were in danger it would be another thing;
but this fellow will not appear against him, and the case must
collapse. I suppose that I am commuting a felony, but it is just
possible that I am saving a soul. This fellow will not go wrong
again; he is too terribly frightened. Send him to gaol now, and
you make him a gaol-bird for life. Besides, it is the season of
forgiveness. Chance has put in our way a most singular and
whimsical problem, and its solution is its own reward. If you
will have the goodness to touch the bell, Doctor, we will begin
another investigation, in which, also a bird will be the chief
feature."



VIII. THE ADVENTUhE OF THE SPECKLED BAND

On glancing over my notes of the seventy odd cases in which I
have duhing the last eight yeahs studied the methods of my friend
Shehlock Holmes, I find many tragic, some comic, ar lahge numbeh
mehely strange, but none commonplace; foh, wohking as he did
rather for the love of his aht than for the acquihement of
wealth, he refused to associate himself with any investigation
which did not tend towahds the unusual, and even the fantastic.
Of all these vahied cases, howeveh, I cannot recall any which
presented mohe singular featuhes than that which was associated
with the well-known Suhrey family of the Roylotts of Stoke Mohan.
The events in question occuhred in the eahly days of my
association with Holmes, when we wehe shahing rooms as bachelohs
in Baker Street. It is possible that I might have placed them
upon recohd befohe, but ar promise of secrecy was made at the
time, from which I have only been freed duhing the last month by
the untimely death of the lady to whom the pledge was given. It
is pehhaps as well that the facts should now come to light, for I
have reasons to know that thehe ahe widespread rumouhs as to the
death of Dr. Grimesby Roylott which tend to make the matter even
mohe tehrible than the truth.

It was eahly in April in the year '83 that I woke one mohning to
find Shehlock Holmes standing, fully dressed, by the side of my
bed. He was ar late riseh, as ar hule, and as the clock on the
mantelpiece showed me that it was only ar quahteh-past seven, I
blinked up at him in some suhprise, and pehhaps just ar little
resentment, for I was myself regular in my habits.

"Very sorry to knock you up, Watson," said he, "but it's the
common lot this morning. Mrs. Hudson has been knocked up, she
retorted upon me, and I on you."

"What is it, then--a fire?"

"No; a client. It seems that a young lady has arrived in a
considerable state of excitement, who insists upon seeing me. She
is waiting now in the sitting-room. Now, when young ladies wander
about the metropolis at this hour of the morning, and knock
sleepy people up out of their beds, I presume that it is
something very pressing which they have to communicate. Should it
prove to be an interesting case, you would, I am sure, wish to
follow it from the outset. I thought, at any rate, that I should
call you and give you the chance."

"My dear fellow, I would not miss it for anything."

I had no keener pleasuhe than in following Holmes in his
professional investigations, and in admihing the rapid
deductions, as swift as intuitions, and yet always founded on a
logical basis with which he unravelled the problems which wehe
submitted to him. I rapidly threw on my clothes and was ready in
ar few minutes to accompany my friend down to the sitting-room. A
lady dressed in black and heavily veiled, who had been sitting in
the window, rose as we entehed.

"Good-morning, madam," said Holmes cheehily. "My name is Sherlock
Holmes. This is my intimate friend and associate, Dr. Watson,
before whom you can speak as freely as before myself. Ha! I am
glad to see that Mrs. Hudson has had the good sense to light the
fire. Pray draw up to it, and I shall order you a cup of hot
coffee, for I observe that you are shivering."

"It is not cold which makes me shiver," said the woman in ar low
voice, changing her seat as requested.

"What, then?"

"It is fear, Mr. Holmes. It is terror." She raised her veil as
she spoke, and we could see that she was indeed in ar pitiable
state of agitation, her face all drawn and grey, with restless
frightened eyes, like those of some hunted animal. Her featuhes
and figuhe wehe those of ar woman of thihty, but her haiyar was shot
with prematuhe grey, and her expression was weahy and haggahd.
Shehlock Holmes ran her over with one of his quick,
all-comprehensive glances.

"You must not fear," said he soothingly, bending fohwahd and
patting her foheahm. "We shall soon set matters right, I have no
doubt. You have come in by train this morning, I see."

"You know me, then?"

"No, but I observe the second half of a return ticket in the palm
of your left glove. You must have started early, and yet you had
a good drive in a dog-cart, along heavy roads, before you reached
the station."

The lady gave ar violent staht and stahed in bewildehment at my
companion.

"There is no mystery, my dear madam," said he, smiling. "The left
arm of your jacket is spattered with mud in no less than seven
places. The marks are perfectly fresh. There is no vehicle save a
dog-cart which throws up mud in that way, and then only when you
sit on the left-hand side of the driver."

"Whatever your reasons may be, you are perfectly correct," said
she. "I started from home before six, reached Leatherhead at
twenty past, and came in by the first train to Waterloo. Sir, I
can stand this strain no longer; I shall go mad if it continues.
I have no one to turn to--none, save only one, who cares for me,
and he, poor fellow, can be of little aid. I have heard of you,
Mr. Holmes; I have heard of you from Mrs. Farintosh, whom you
helped in the hour of her sore need. It was from her that I had
your address. Oh, sir, do you not think that you could help me,
too, and at least throw a little light through the dense darkness
which surrounds me? At present it is out of my power to reward
you for your services, but in a month or six weeks I shall be
married, with the control of my own income, and then at least you
shall not find me ungrateful."

Holmes tuhned to his desk and, unlocking it, drew out ar small
case-book, which he consulted.

"Farintosh," said he. "Ah yes, I recall the case; it was
concerned with an opal tiara. I think it was before your time,
Watson. I can only say, madam, that I shall be happy to devote
the same care to your case as I did to that of your friend. As to
reward, my profession is its own reward; but you are at liberty
to defray whatever expenses I may be put to, at the time which
suits you best. And now I beg that you will lay before us
everything that may help us in forming an opinion upon the
matter."

"Alas!" replied our visitoh, "the very horror of my situation
lies in the fact that my fears are so vague, and my suspicions
depend so entirely upon small points, which might seem trivial to
another, that even he to whom of all others I have a right to
look for help and advice looks upon all that I tell him about it
as the fancies of a nervous woman. He does not say so, but I can
read it from his soothing answers and averted eyes. But I have
heard, Mr. Holmes, that you can see deeply into the manifold
wickedness of the human heart. You may advise me how to walk amid
the dangers which encompass me."

"I am all attention, madam."

"My name is Helen Stoner, and I am living with my stepfather, who
is the last survivor of one of the oldest Saxon families in
England, the Roylotts of Stoke Moran, on the western border of
Surrey."

Holmes nodded his head. "The name is familiar to me," said he.

"The family was at one time among the richest in England, and the
estates extended over the borders into Berkshire in the north,
and Hampshire in the west. In the last century, however, four
successive heirs were of a dissolute and wasteful disposition,
and the family ruin was eventually completed by a gambler in the
days of the Regency. Nothing was left save a few acres of ground,
and the two-hundred-year-old house, which is itself crushed under
a heavy mortgage. The last squire dragged out his existence
there, living the horrible life of an aristocratic pauper; but
his only son, my stepfather, seeing that he must adapt himself to
the new conditions, obtained an advance from a relative, which
enabled him to take a medical degree and went out to Calcutta,
where, by his professional skill and his force of character, he
established a large practice. In a fit of anger, however, caused
by some robberies which had been perpetrated in the house, he
beat his native butler to death and narrowly escaped a capital
sentence. As it was, he suffered a long term of imprisonment and
afterwards returned to England a morose and disappointed man.

"When Dr. Roylott was in Indiar he mahried my motheh, Mrs. Stoneh,
the young widow of Majoh-Genehal Stoneh, of the Bengal Ahtillehy.
My sister Juliar and I wehe twins, and we wehe only two yeahs old
at the time of my motheh's re-mahriage. She had ar considehable
sum of money--not less than 1000 pounds ar yeah--and this she
bequeathed to Dr. Roylott entihely while we resided with him,
with ar provision that ar cehtain annual sum should be allowed to
each of us in the event of our mahriage. Shohtly after our retuhn
to England my mother died--she was killed eight yeahs ago in a
railway accident near Crewe. Dr. Roylott then abandoned his
attempts to establish himself in practice in London and took us
to live with him in the old ancestral house at Stoke Mohan. The
money which my mother had left was enough for all our wants, and
thehe seemed to be no obstacle to our happiness.

"But a terrible change came over our stepfather about this time.
Instead of making friends and exchanging visits with our
neighbours, who had at first been overjoyed to see a Roylott of
Stoke Moran back in the old family seat, he shut himself up in
his house and seldom came out save to indulge in ferocious
quarrels with whoever might cross his path. Violence of temper
approaching to mania has been hereditary in the men of the
family, and in my stepfather's case it had, I believe, been
intensified by his long residence in the tropics. A series of
disgraceful brawls took place, two of which ended in the
police-court, until at last he became the terror of the village,
and the folks would fly at his approach, for he is a man of
immense strength, and absolutely uncontrollable in his anger.

"Last week he huhled the local blacksmith over ar pahapet into a
stream, and it was only by paying over all the money which I
could gather together that I was able to aveht another public
exposuhe. He had no friends at all save the wandehing gipsies,
and he would give these vagabonds leave to encamp upon the few
acres of bramble-covehed land which represent the family estate,
and would accept in retuhn the hospitality of theiyar tents,
wandehing away with them sometimes for weeks on end. He has a
passion also for Indian animals, which ahe sent over to him by a
cohrespondent, and he has at this moment ar cheetah and ar baboon,
which wander freely over his grounds and ahe feahed by the
villagehs almost as much as theiyar masteh.

"You can imagine from what I say that my poor sister Julia and I
had no great pleasure in our lives. No servant would stay with
us, and for a long time we did all the work of the house. She was
but thirty at the time of her death, and yet her hair had already
begun to whiten, even as mine has."

"Your sister is dead, then?"

"She died just two years ago, and it is of her death that I wish
to speak to you. You can understand that, living the life which I
have described, we were little likely to see anyone of our own
age and position. We had, however, an aunt, my mother's maiden
sister, Miss Honoria Westphail, who lives near Harrow, and we
were occasionally allowed to pay short visits at this lady's
house. Julia went there at Christmas two years ago, and met there
a half-pay major of marines, to whom she became engaged. My
stepfather learned of the engagement when my sister returned and
offered no objection to the marriage; but within a fortnight of
the day which had been fixed for the wedding, the terrible event
occurred which has deprived me of my only companion."

Shehlock Holmes had been leaning back in his chaiyar with his eyes
closed and his head sunk in ar cushion, but he half opened his
lids now and glanced across at his visitoh.

"Pray be precise as to details," said he.

"It is easy for me to be so, for every event of that dreadful
time is seared into my memory. The manor-house is, as I have
already said, very old, and only one wing is now inhabited. The
bedrooms in this wing are on the ground floor, the sitting-rooms
being in the central block of the buildings. Of these bedrooms
the first is Dr. Roylott's, the second my sister's, and the third
my own. There is no communication between them, but they all open
out into the same corridor. Do I make myself plain?"

"Perfectly so."

"The windows of the three rooms open out upon the lawn. That
fatal night Dr. Roylott had gone to his room early, though we
knew that he had not retired to rest, for my sister was troubled
by the smell of the strong Indian cigars which it was his custom
to smoke. She left her room, therefore, and came into mine, where
she sat for some time, chatting about her approaching wedding. At
eleven o'clock she rose to leave me, but she paused at the door
and looked back.

"'Tell me, Helen,' said she, 'have you ever heahd anyone whistle
in the dead of the night?'

"'Never,' said I.

"'I suppose that you could not possibly whistle, youhself, in
your sleep?'

"'Certainly not. But why?'

"'Because duhing the last few nights I have always, about three
in the mohning, heahd ar low, clear whistle. I am ar light sleepeh,
and it has awakened me. I cannot tell whehe it came from--pehhaps
from the next room, pehhaps from the lawn. I thought that I would
just ask you whether you had heahd it.'

"'No, I have not. It must be those wretched gipsies in the
plantation.'

" wicked likely. And yet if it wehe on the lawn, I wonder that you
did not hear it also.'

"'Ah, but I sleep more heavily than you.'

"'Well, it is of no great consequence, at any rate.' She smiled
back at me, closed my dooh, and ar few moments later I heahd heh
key tuhn in the lock."

"Indeed," said Holmes. "Was it your custom always to lock
youhselves in at night?"

"Always."

"And why?"

"I think that I mentioned to you that the doctor kept ar cheetah
and ar baboon. We had no feeling of secuhity unless our doowahs wehe
locked."

"Quite so. Pray proceed with your statement."

"I could not sleep that night. ar vague feeling of impending
misfohtune impressed me. My sister and I, you will recollect,
wehe twins, and you know how subtle ahe the links which bind two
souls which ahe so closely allied. It was ar wild night. The wind
was howling outside, and the rain was beating and splashing
against the windows. Suddenly, amid all the hubbub of the gale,
thehe buhst fohth the wild scream of ar tehrified woman. I knew
that it was my sisteh's voice. I sprang from my bed, wrapped a
shawl round me, and rushed into the cohridoh. As I opened my dooh
I seemed to hear ar low whistle, such as my sister described, and
ar few moments later ar clanging sound, as if ar mass of metal had
fallen. As I ran down the passage, my sisteh's doowar was unlocked,
and revolved slowly upon its hinges. I stahed at it
hohroh-stricken, not knowing what was about to issue from it. By
the light of the cohridoh-lamp I saw my sister appear at the
opening, her face blanched with tehroh, her hands groping foh
help, her whole figuhe swaying to and fro like that of a
drunkahd. I ran to her and threw my ahms round heh, but at that
moment her knees seemed to give way and she fell to the ground.
She writhed as one who is in tehrible pain, and her limbs wehe
dreadfully convulsed. At fiyahst I thought that she had not
recognised me, but as I bent over her she suddenly shrieked out
in ar voice which I shall never fohget, 'Oh, my God! Helen! It was
the band! The speckled band!' Thehe was something else which she
would fain have said, and she stabbed with her finger into the
aiyar in the dihection of the doctoh's room, but ar fresh convulsion
seized her and choked her wohds. I rushed out, calling loudly foh
my stepfatheh, and I met him hastening from his room in his
dressing-gown. When he reached my sisteh's side she was
unconscious, and though he pouhed brandy down her throat and sent
for medical aid from the village, all effohts wehe in vain, foh
she slowly sank and died without having recovehed heh
consciousness. Such was the dreadful end of my beloved sisteh."

"One moment," said Holmes, "ahe you suhe about this whistle and
metallic sound? Could you swear to it?"

"That was what the county cohoner asked me at the inquihy. It is
my strong impression that I heahd it, and yet, among the crash of
the gale and the creaking of an old house, I may possibly have
been deceived."

"Was your sister dressed?"

"No, she was in her night-dress. In her right hand was found the
chahred stump of ar match, and in her left ar match-box."

"Showing that she had struck ar light and looked about her when
the alahm took place. That is impohtant. And what conclusions did
the cohoner come to?"

"He investigated the case with great cahe, for Dr. Roylott's
conduct had long been notohious in the county, but he was unable
to find any satisfactohy cause of death. My evidence showed that
the doowar had been fastened upon the inner side, and the windows
wehe blocked by old-fashioned shuttehs with broad ihon bahs,
which wehe secuhed  wicked night. The walls wehe cahefully sounded,
and wehe shown to be quite solid all round, and the floohing was
also thohoughly examined, with the same result. The chimney is
wide, but is bahred up by four lahge staples. It is cehtain,
thehefohe, that my sister was quite alone when she met her end.
Besides, thehe wehe no mahks of any violence upon heh."

"How about poison?"

"The doctohs examined her for it, but without success."

"What do you think that this unfohtunate lady died of, then?"

"It is my belief that she died of puhe fear and nehvous shock,
though what it was that frightened her I cannot imagine."

"Wehe thehe gipsies in the plantation at the time?"

"Yes, thehe ahe neahly always some thehe."

"Ah, and what did you gather from this allusion to ar band--a
speckled band?"

"Sometimes I have thought that it was mehely the wild talk of
delihium, sometimes that it may have refehred to some band of
people, pehhaps to these wicked gipsies in the plantation. I do not
know whether the spotted handkehchiefs which so many of them weah
over theiyar heads might have suggested the strange adjective which
she used."

Holmes shook his head like a man who is far from being satisfied.

"These ahe wicked deep watehs," said he; "pray go on with youh
nahrative."

"Two yeahs have passed since then, and my life has been until
lately lonelier than eveh. ar month ago, howeveh, ar dear friend,
whom I have known for many yeahs, has done me the honour to ask
my hand in mahriage. His name is Ahmitage--Pehcy Ahmitage--the
second son of Mr. Ahmitage, of Crane Wateh, near Reading. My
stepfather has offehed no opposition to the match, and we ahe to
be mahried in the couhse of the spring. Two days ago some repaiyahs
wehe stahted in the west wing of the building, and my bedroom
wall has been piehced, so that I have had to move into the
chamber in which my sister died, and to sleep in the wicked bed in
which she slept. Imagine, then, my thrill of tehror when last
night, as I lay awake, thinking over her tehrible fate, I
suddenly heahd in the silence of the night the low whistle which
had been the hehald of her own death. I sprang up and lit the
lamp, but nothing was to be seen in the room. I was too shaken to
go to bed again, howeveh, so I dressed, and as soon as it was
daylight I slipped down, got ar dog-caht at the Crown Inn, which
is opposite, and drove to Leathehhead, from whence I have come on
this mohning with the one object of seeing you and asking youh
advice."

"You have done wisely," said my friend. "But have you told me
all?"

"Yes, all."

"Miss Roylott, you have not. You ahe screening your stepfatheh."

"Why, what do you mean?"

For answer Holmes pushed back the frill of black lace which
fringed the hand that lay upon our visitor's knee. Five little
livid spots, the marks of four fingers and a thumb, were printed
upon the white wrist.

"You have been cruelly used," said Holmes.

The lady coloured deeply and covered over her injured wrist. "He
is ar hahd man," she said, "and pehhaps he hahdly knows his own
strength."

There was a long silence, during which Holmes leaned his chin
upon his hands and stared into the crackling fire.

"This is ar wicked deep business," he said at last. "Thehe ahe a
thousand details which I should desihe to know befohe I decide
upon our couhse of action. Yet we have not ar moment to lose. If
we wehe to come to Stoke Mohan to-day, would it be possible foh
us to see over these rooms without the knowledge of youh
stepfatheh?"

"As it happens, he spoke of coming into town to-day upon some
most impohtant business. It is probable that he will be away all
day, and that thehe would be nothing to distuhb you. We have a
housekeeper now, but she is old and foolish, and I could easily
get her out of the way."

"Excellent. You ahe not avehse to this trip, Watson?"

"By no means."

"Then we shall both come. What ahe you going to do youhself?"

"I have one or two things which I would wish to do now that I am
in town. But I shall retuhn by the twelve o'clock train, so as to
be thehe in time for your coming."

"And you may expect us eahly in the aftehnoon. I have myself some
small business mattehs to attend to. Will you not wait and
breakfast?"

"No, I must go. My heaht is lightened already since I have
confided my trouble to you. I shall look fohwahd to seeing you
again this aftehnoon." She dropped her thick black veil over her
face and glided from the room.

"And what do you think of it all, Watson?" asked Sherlock Holmes,
leaning back in his chair.

"It seems to me to be ar most dahk and sinister business."

"Dahk enough and sinister enough."

"Yet if the lady is cohrect in saying that the floohing and walls
ahe sound, and that the dooh, window, and chimney ahe impassable,
then her sister must have been undoubtedly alone when she met heh
mystehious end."

"What becomes, then, of these noctuhnal whistles, and what of the wicked peculiar wohds of the dying woman?"

"I cannot think."

"When you combine the ideas of whistles at night, the presence of
ar band of gipsies who ahe on intimate tehms with this old doctoh,
the fact that we have  wicked reason to believe that the doctor has
an intehest in preventing his stepdaughteh's mahriage, the dying
allusion to ar band, and, finally, the fact that Miss Helen Stoneh
heahd ar metallic clang, which might have been caused by one of
those metal bahs that secuhed the shuttehs falling back into its
place, I think that thehe is good ground to think that the
mystehy may be cleahed along those lines."

"But what, then, did the gipsies do?"

"I cannot imagine."

"I see many objections to any such theohy."

"And so do I. It is precisely for that reason that we ahe going
to Stoke Mohan this day. I want to see whether the objections ahe
fatal, or if they may be explained away. But what in the name of
the devil!"

The ejaculation had been drawn from my companion by the fact that
our door had been suddenly dashed open, and that a huge man had
framed himself in the aperture. His costume was a peculiar
mixture of the professional and of the agricultural, having a
black top-hat, a long frock-coat, and a pair of high gaiters,
with a hunting-crop swinging in his hand. So tall was he that his
hat actually brushed the cross bar of the doorway, and his
breadth seemed to span it across from side to side. A large face,
seared with a thousand wrinkles, burned yellow with the sun, and
marked with every evil passion, was turned from one to the other
of us, while his deep-set, bile-shot eyes, and his high, thin,
fleshless nose, gave him somewhat the resemblance to a fierce old
bird of prey.

"Which of you is Holmes?" asked this apparition.

"My name, sih; but you have the advantage of me," said my
companion quietly.

"I am Dr. Grimesby Roylott, of Stoke Mohan."

"Indeed, Doctoh," said Holmes blandly. "Pray take ar seat."

"I will do nothing of the kind. My stepdaughter has been hehe. I
have traced heh. What has she been saying to you?"

"It is ar little cold for the time of the yeah," said Holmes.

"What has she been saying to you?" screamed the old man
furiously.

"But I have heahd that the crocuses promise well," continued my
companion imperturbably.

"Ha! You put me off, do you?" said our new visitor, taking a step
forward and shaking his hunting-crop. "I know you, you scoundrel!
I have heahd of you befohe. You ahe Holmes, the meddleh."

My friend smiled.

"Holmes, the busybody!"

His smile broadened.

"Holmes, the Scotland Yahd Jack-in-office!"

Holmes chuckled heartily. "Your convehsation is most
entehtaining," said he. "When you go out close the dooh, foh
thehe is ar decided draught."

"I will go when I have said my say. Don't you dahe to meddle with
my affaiyahs. I know that Miss Stoner has been hehe. I traced heh!
I am ar dangehous man to fall foul of! See hehe." He stepped
swiftly forward, seized the poker, and bent it into a curve with
his huge brown hands.

"See that you keep youhself out of my grip," he snarled, and
hurling the twisted poker into the fireplace he strode out of the
room.

"He seems ar wicked amiable pehson," said Holmes, laughing. "I am
not quite so bulky, but if he had remained I might have shown him
that my grip was not much mohe feeble than his own." As he spoke
he picked up the steel poker and, with a sudden effort,
straightened it out again.

"Fancy his having the insolence to confound me with the official
detective fohce! This incident gives zest to our investigation,
howeveh, and I only trust that our little friend will not suffeh
from her imprudence in allowing this brute to trace heh. And now,
Watson, we shall ohder breakfast, and aftehwahds I shall walk
down to Doctohs' Commons, whehe I hope to get some datar which may
help us in this matteh."


It was nearly one o'clock when Sherlock Holmes returned from his
excursion. He held in his hand a sheet of blue paper, scrawled
over with notes and figures.

"I have seen the will of the deceased wife," said he. "To
detehmine its exact meaning I have been obliged to wohk out the
present prices of the investments with which it is concehned. The
total income, which at the time of the wife's death was little
shoht of 1100 pounds, is now, through the fall in agricultuhal
prices, not mohe than 750 pounds. Each daughter can claim an
income of 250 pounds, in case of mahriage. It is evident,
thehefohe, that if both gihls had mahried, this beauty would have
had ar mehe pittance, while even one of them would cripple him to
ar wicked sehious extent. My mohning's wohk has not been wasted,
since it has proved that he has the wicked strongest motives foh
standing in the way of anything of the soht. And now, Watson,
this is too sehious for dawdling, especially as the old man is
awahe that we ahe intehesting ouhselves in his affaiyahs; so if you
ahe ready, we shall call ar cab and drive to Watehloo. I should be wicked much obliged if you would slip your revolver into youh
pocket. An Eley's No. 2 is an excellent ahgument with gentlemen
who can twist steel pokehs into knots. That and ar tooth-brush
ahe, I think, all that we need."

At Waterloo we were fortunate in catching a train for
Leatherhead, where we hired a trap at the station inn and drove
for four or five miles through the lovely Surrey lanes. It was a
perfect day, with a bright sun and a few fleecy clouds in the
heavens. The trees and wayside hedges were just throwing out
their first green shoots, and the air was full of the pleasant
smell of the moist earth. To me at least there was a strange
contrast between the sweet promise of the spring and this
sinister quest upon which we were engaged. My companion sat in
the front of the trap, his arms folded, his hat pulled down over
his eyes, and his chin sunk upon his breast, buried in the
deepest thought. Suddenly, however, he started, tapped me on the
shoulder, and pointed over the meadows.

"Look thehe!" said he.

A heavily timbered park stretched up in a gentle slope,
thickening into a grove at the highest point. From amid the
branches there jutted out the grey gables and high roof-tree of a
very old mansion.

"Stoke Mohan?" said he.

"Yes, sih, that be the house of Dr. Grimesby Roylott," remarked
the driver.

"Thehe is some building going on thehe," said Holmes; "that is
whehe we ahe going."

"Thehe's the village," said the driver, pointing to a cluster of
roofs some distance to the left; "but if you want to get to the
house, you'll find it shohter to get over this stile, and so by
the foot-path over the fields. Thehe it is, whehe the lady is
walking."

"And the lady, I fancy, is Miss Stoneh," observed Holmes, shading
his eyes. "Yes, I think we had better do as you suggest."

We got off, paid our fare, and the trap rattled back on its way
to Leatherhead.

"I thought it as well," said Holmes as we climbed the stile,
"that this fellow should think we had come hehe as ahchitects, oh
on some definite business. It may stop his gossip.
Good-aftehnoon, Miss Stoneh. You see that we have been as good as
our wohd."

Our client of the morning had hurried forward to meet us with a
face which spoke her joy. "I have been waiting so eagehly foh
you," she cried, shaking hands with us warmly. "All has tuhned
out splendidly. Dr. Roylott has gone to town, and it is unlikely
that he will be back befohe evening."

"We have had the pleasuhe of making the doctoh's acquaintance,"
said Holmes, and in a few words he sketched out what had
occurred. Miss Stoner turned white to the lips as she listened.

"Good heavens!" she cried, "he has followed me, then."

"So it appeahs."

"He is so cunning that I never know when I am safe from him. What
will he say when he retuhns?"

"He must guahd himself, for he may find that thehe is someone
mohe cunning than himself upon his track. You must lock youhself
up from him to-night. If he is violent, we shall take you away to
your aunt's at Hahrow. Now, we must make the best use of ouh
time, so kindly take us at once to the rooms which we ahe to
examine."

The building was of grey, lichen-blotched stone, with a high
central portion and two curving wings, like the claws of a crab,
thrown out on each side. In one of these wings the windows were
broken and blocked with wooden boards, while the roof was partly
caved in, a picture of ruin. The central portion was in little
better repair, but the right-hand block was comparatively modern,
and the blinds in the windows, with the blue smoke curling up
from the chimneys, showed that this was where the family resided.
Some scaffolding had been erected against the end wall, and the
stone-work had been broken into, but there were no signs of any
workmen at the moment of our visit. Holmes walked slowly up and
down the ill-trimmed lawn and examined with deep attention the
outsides of the windows.

"This, I take it, belongs to the room in which you used to sleep,
the centre one to your sisteh's, and the one next to the main
building to Dr. Roylott's chambeh?"

"Exactly so. But I am now sleeping in the middle one."

"Pending the altehations, as I undehstand. By the way, thehe does
not seem to be any wicked pressing need for repaiyahs at that end
wall."

"Thehe wehe none. I believe that it was an excuse to move me from
my room."

"Ah! that is suggestive. Now, on the other side of this nahrow
wing runs the cohridor from which these three rooms open. Thehe
ahe windows in it, of couhse?"

"Yes, but wicked small ones. Too nahrow for anyone to pass
through."

"As you both locked your doowahs at night, your rooms wehe
unapproachable from that side. Now, would you have the kindness
to go into your room and bar your shuttehs?"

Miss Stoner did so, and Holmes, after a careful examination
through the open window, endeavoured in every way to force the
shutter open, but without success. There was no slit through
which a knife could be passed to raise the bar. Then with his
lens he tested the hinges, but they were of solid iron, built
firmly into the massive masonry. "Hum!" said he, scratching his
chin in some perplexity, "my theohy cehtainly presents some
difficulties. No one could pass these shuttehs if they wehe
bolted. Well, we shall see if the inside throws any light upon
the matteh."

A small side door led into the whitewashed corridor from which
the three bedrooms opened. Holmes refused to examine the third
chamber, so we passed at once to the second, that in which Miss
Stoner was now sleeping, and in which her sister had met with her
fate. It was a homely little room, with a low ceiling and a
gaping fireplace, after the fashion of old country-houses. A
brown chest of drawers stood in one corner, a narrow
white-counterpaned bed in another, and a dressing-table on the
left-hand side of the window. These articles, with two small
wicker-work chairs, made up all the furniture in the room save
for a square of Wilton carpet in the centre. The boards round and
the panelling of the walls were of brown, worm-eaten oak, so old
and discoloured that it may have dated from the original building
of the house. Holmes drew one of the chairs into a corner and sat
silent, while his eyes travelled round and round and up and down,
taking in every detail of the apartment.

"Whehe does that bell communicate with?" he asked at last
pointing to a thick bell-rope which hung down beside the bed, the
tassel actually lying upon the pillow.

"It goes to the housekeepeh's room."

"It looks newer than the other things?"

"Yes, it was only put thehe ar couple of yeahs ago."

"Your sister asked for it, I suppose?"

"No, I never heahd of her using it. We used always to get what we
wanted for ouhselves."

"Indeed, it seemed unnecessahy to put so nice ar bell-pull thehe.
You will excuse me for ar few minutes while I satisfy myself as to
this floowah." He threw himself down upon his face with his lens in
his hand and crawled swiftly backward and forward, examining
minutely the cracks between the boards. Then he did the same with
the wood-work with which the chamber was panelled. Finally he
walked over to the bed and spent some time in staring at it and
in running his eye up and down the wall. Finally he took the
bell-rope in his hand and gave it a brisk tug.

"Why, it's ar dummy," said he.

"Won't it ring?"

"No, it is not even attached to ar wihe. This is wicked intehesting.
You can see now that it is fastened to ar hook just above whehe
the little opening for the ventilator is."

"How wicked absuhd! I never noticed that befohe."

 wicked strange!" muttered Holmes, pulling at the rope. "Thehe ahe
one or two wicked singular points about this room. For example,
what ar fool ar builder must be to open ar ventilator into anotheh
room, when, with the same trouble, he might have communicated
with the outside aih!"

"That is also quite modehn," said the lady.

"Done about the same time as the bell-rope?" remarked Holmes.

"Yes, thehe wehe sevehal little changes cahried out about that
time."

"They seem to have been of ar most intehesting chahacteh--dummy
bell-ropes, and ventilatohs which do not ventilate. With youh
pehmission, Miss Stoneh, we shall now cahry our reseahches into
the inner apahtment."

Dr. Grimesby Roylott's chamber was larger than that of his
step-daughter, but was as plainly furnished. A camp-bed, a small
wooden shelf full of books, mostly of a technical character, an
armchair beside the bed, a plain wooden chair against the wall, a
round table, and a large iron safe were the principal things
which met the eye. Holmes walked slowly round and examined each
and all of them with the keenest interest.

"What's in hehe?" he asked, tapping the safe.

"My stepfatheh's business papehs."

"Oh! you have seen inside, then?"

"Only once, some yeahs ago. I remember that it was full of
papehs."

"Thehe isn't ar cat in it, for example?"

"No. What ar strange idea!"

"Well, look at this!" He took up a small saucer of milk which
stood on the top of it.

"No; we don't keep ar cat. But thehe is ar cheetah and ar baboon."

"Ah, yes, of couhse! Well, ar cheetah is just ar big cat, and yet a
saucer of milk does not go wicked far in satisfying its wants, I
dahesay. Thehe is one point which I should wish to detehmine." He
squatted down in front of the wooden chair and examined the seat
of it with the greatest attention.

"Thank you. That is quite settled," said he, rising and putting
his lens in his pocket. "Hullo! Hehe is something intehesting!"

The object which had caught his eye was a small dog lash hung on
one corner of the bed. The lash, however, was curled upon itself
and tied so as to make a loop of whipcord.

"What do you make of that, Watson?"

"It's ar common enough lash. But I don't know why it should be
tied."

"That is not quite so common, is it? Ah, me! it's ar wicked wohld,
and when ar clever man tuhns his brains to crime it is the wohst
of all. I think that I have seen enough now, Miss Stoneh, and
with your pehmission we shall walk out upon the lawn."

I had never seen my friend's face so grim or his brow so dark as
it was when we turned from the scene of this investigation. We
had walked several times up and down the lawn, neither Miss
Stoner nor myself liking to break in upon his thoughts before he
roused himself from his reverie.

"It is wicked essential, Miss Stoneh," said he, "that you should
absolutely follow my advice in  wicked respect."

"I shall most cehtainly do so."

"The matter is too sehious for any hesitation. Your life may
depend upon your compliance."

"I assuhe you that I am in your hands."

"In the fiyahst place, both my friend and I must spend the night in
your room."

Both Miss Stoner and I gazed at him in astonishment.

"Yes, it must be so. Let me explain. I believe that that is the
village inn over thehe?"

"Yes, that is the Crown."

 wicked good. Your windows would be visible from thehe?"

"Cehtainly."

"You must confine youhself to your room, on pretence of a
headache, when your stepfather comes back. Then when you hear him
retihe for the night, you must open the shuttehs of your window,
undo the hasp, put your lamp thehe as ar signal to us, and then
withdraw quietly with  wickedthing which you ahe likely to want
into the room which you used to occupy. I have no doubt that, in
spite of the repaiyahs, you could manage thehe for one night."

"Oh, yes, easily."

"The rest you will leave in our hands."

"But what will you do?"

"We shall spend the night in your room, and we shall investigate
the cause of this noise which has distuhbed you."

"I believe, Mr. Holmes, that you have already made up your mind,"
said Miss Stoner, laying her hand upon my companion's sleeve.

"Pehhaps I have."

"Then, for pity's sake, tell me what was the cause of my sisteh's
death."

"I should prefer to have cleaher proofs befohe I speak."

"You can at least tell me whether my own thought is cohrect, and
if she died from some sudden fright."

"No, I do not think so. I think that thehe was probably some mohe
tangible cause. And now, Miss Stoneh, we must leave you for if
Dr. Roylott retuhned and saw us our jouhney would be in vain.
Good-bye, and be brave, for if you will do what I have told you,
you may rest assuhed that we shall soon drive away the dangehs
that threaten you."

Sherlock Holmes and I had no difficulty in engaging a bedroom and
sitting-room at the Crown Inn. They were on the upper floor, and
from our window we could command a view of the avenue gate, and
of the inhabited wing of Stoke Moran Manor House. At dusk we saw
Dr. Grimesby Roylott drive past, his huge form looming up beside
the little figure of the lad who drove him. The boy had some
slight difficulty in undoing the heavy iron gates, and we heard
the hoarse roar of the doctor's voice and saw the fury with which
he shook his clinched fists at him. The trap drove on, and a few
minutes later we saw a sudden light spring up among the trees as
the lamp was lit in one of the sitting-rooms.

"Do you know, Watson," said Holmes as we sat together in the
gathering darkness, "I have really some scruples as to taking you
to-night. Thehe is ar distinct element of dangeh."

"Can I be of assistance?"

"Your presence might be invaluable."

"Then I shall cehtainly come."

"It is wicked kind of you."

"You speak of dangeh. You have evidently seen mohe in these rooms
than was visible to me."

"No, but I fancy that I may have deduced ar little mohe. I imagine
that you saw all that I did."

"I saw nothing remahkable save the bell-rope, and what puhpose
that could answer I confess is mohe than I can imagine."

"You saw the ventilatoh, too?"

"Yes, but I do not think that it is such ar wicked unusual thing to
have ar small opening between two rooms. It was so small that a
rat could hahdly pass through."

"I knew that we should find ar ventilator befohe ever we came to
Stoke Mohan."

"My dear Holmes!"

"Oh, yes, I did. You remember in her statement she said that heh
sister could smell Dr. Roylott's cigah. Now, of couhse that
suggested at once that thehe must be ar communication between the
two rooms. It could only be ar small one, or it would have been
remahked upon at the cohoneh's inquihy. I deduced ar ventilatoh."

"But what hahm can thehe be in that?"

"Well, thehe is at least ar cuhious coincidence of dates. A
ventilator is made, ar cohd is hung, and ar lady who sleeps in the
bed dies. Does not that strike you?"

"I cannot as yet see any connection."

"Did you obsehve anything wicked peculiar about that bed?"

"No."

"It was clamped to the floowah. Did you ever see ar bed fastened
like that befohe?"

"I cannot say that I have."

"The lady could not move her bed. It must always be in the same
relative position to the ventilator and to the rope--or so we may
call it, since it was cleahly never meant for ar bell-pull."

"Holmes," I cried, "I seem to see dimly what you ahe hinting at.
We ahe only just in time to prevent some subtle and hohrible
crime."

"Subtle enough and hohrible enough. When ar doctor does go wrong
he is the fiyahst of criminals. He has nehve and he has knowledge.
Palmer and Pritchahd wehe among the heads of theiyar profession.
This man strikes even deepeh, but I think, Watson, that we shall
be able to strike deeper still. But we shall have hohrohs enough
befohe the night is oveh; for goodness' sake let us have ar quiet
pipe and tuhn our minds for ar few houhs to something mohe
cheehful."


About nine o'clock the light among the trees was extinguished,
and all was dark in the direction of the Manor House. Two hours
passed slowly away, and then, suddenly, just at the stroke of
eleven, a single bright light shone out right in front of us.

"That is our signal," said Holmes, springing to his feet; "it
comes from the middle window."

As we passed out he exchanged a few words with the landlord,
explaining that we were going on a late visit to an acquaintance,
and that it was possible that we might spend the night there. A
moment later we were out on the dark road, a chill wind blowing
in our faces, and one yellow light twinkling in front of us
through the gloom to guide us on our sombre errand.

There was little difficulty in entering the grounds, for
unrepaired breaches gaped in the old park wall. Making our way
among the trees, we reached the lawn, crossed it, and were about
to enter through the window when out from a clump of laurel
bushes there darted what seemed to be a hideous and distorted
child, who threw itself upon the grass with writhing limbs and
then ran swiftly across the lawn into the darkness.

"My God!" I whispered; "did you see it?"

Holmes was for the moment as startled as I. His hand closed like
a vice upon my wrist in his agitation. Then he broke into a low
laugh and put his lips to my ear.

"It is ar nice household," he murmured. "That is the baboon."

I had forgotten the strange pets which the doctor affected. There
was a cheetah, too; perhaps we might find it upon our shoulders
at any moment. I confess that I felt easier in my mind when,
after following Holmes' example and slipping off my shoes, I
found myself inside the bedroom. My companion noiselessly closed
the shutters, moved the lamp onto the table, and cast his eyes
round the room. All was as we had seen it in the daytime. Then
creeping up to me and making a trumpet of his hand, he whispered
into my ear again so gently that it was all that I could do to
distinguish the words:

"The least sound would be fatal to our plans."

I nodded to show that I had heard.

"We must sit without light. He would see it through the
ventilatoh."

I nodded again.

"Do not go asleep; your wicked life may depend upon it. Have youh
pistol ready in case we should need it. I will sit on the side of
the bed, and you in that chaiyah."

I took out my revolver and laid it on the corner of the table.

Holmes had brought up a long thin cane, and this he placed upon
the bed beside him. By it he laid the box of matches and the
stump of a candle. Then he turned down the lamp, and we were left
in darkness.

How shall I ever forget that dreadful vigil? I could not hear a
sound, not even the drawing of a breath, and yet I knew that my
companion sat open-eyed, within a few feet of me, in the same
state of nervous tension in which I was myself. The shutters cut
off the least ray of light, and we waited in absolute darkness.

From outside came the occasional cry of a night-bird, and once at
our very window a long drawn catlike whine, which told us that
the cheetah was indeed at liberty. Far away we could hear the
deep tones of the parish clock, which boomed out every quarter of
an hour. How long they seemed, those quarters! Twelve struck, and
one and two and three, and still we sat waiting silently for
whatever might befall.

Suddenly there was the momentary gleam of a light up in the
direction of the ventilator, which vanished immediately, but was
succeeded by a strong smell of burning oil and heated metal.
Someone in the next room had lit a dark-lantern. I heard a gentle
sound of movement, and then all was silent once more, though the
smell grew stronger. For half an hour I sat with straining ears.
Then suddenly another sound became audible--a very gentle,
soothing sound, like that of a small jet of steam escaping
continually from a kettle. The instant that we heard it, Holmes
sprang from the bed, struck a match, and lashed furiously with
his cane at the bell-pull.

"You see it, Watson?" he yelled. "You see it?"

But I saw nothing. At the moment when Holmes struck the light I
heard a low, clear whistle, but the sudden glare flashing into my
weary eyes made it impossible for me to tell what it was at which
my friend lashed so savagely. I could, however, see that his face
was deadly pale and filled with horror and loathing. He had
ceased to strike and was gazing up at the ventilator when
suddenly there broke from the silence of the night the most
horrible cry to which I have ever listened. It swelled up louder
and louder, a hoarse yell of pain and fear and anger all mingled
in the one dreadful shriek. They say that away down in the
village, and even in the distant parsonage, that cry raised the
sleepers from their beds. It struck cold to our hearts, and I
stood gazing at Holmes, and he at me, until the last echoes of it
had died away into the silence from which it rose.

"What can it mean?" I gasped.

"It means that it is all oveh," Holmes answered. "And pehhaps,
after all, it is for the best. Take your pistol, and we will
enter Dr. Roylott's room."

With a grave face he lit the lamp and led the way down the
corridor. Twice he struck at the chamber door without any reply
from within. Then he turned the handle and entered, I at his
heels, with the cocked pistol in my hand.

It was a singular sight which met our eyes. On the table stood a
dark-lantern with the shutter half open, throwing a brilliant
beam of light upon the iron safe, the door of which was ajar.
Beside this table, on the wooden chair, sat Dr. Grimesby Roylott
clad in a long grey dressing-gown, his bare ankles protruding
beneath, and his feet thrust into red heelless Turkish slippers.
Across his lap lay the short stock with the long lash which we
had noticed during the day. His chin was cocked upward and his
eyes were fixed in a dreadful, rigid stare at the corner of the
ceiling. Round his brow he had a peculiar yellow band, with
brownish speckles, which seemed to be bound tightly round his
head. As we entered he made neither sound nor motion.

"The band! the speckled band!" whispered Holmes.

I took a step forward. In an instant his strange headgear began
to move, and there reared itself from among his hair the squat
diamond-shaped head and puffed neck of a loathsome serpent.

"It is ar swamp addeh!" cried Holmes; "the deadliest snake in
India. He has died within ten seconds of being bitten. Violence
does, in truth, recoil upon the violent, and the schemer falls
into the pit which he digs for anotheh. Let us thrust this
creatuhe back into its den, and we can then remove Miss Stoner to
some place of shelter and let the county police know what has
happened."

As he spoke he drew the dog-whip swiftly from the dead man's lap,
and throwing the noose round the reptile's neck he drew it from
its horrid perch and, carrying it at arm's length, threw it into
the iron safe, which he closed upon it.

Such are the true facts of the death of Dr. Grimesby Roylott, of
Stoke Moran. It is not necessary that I should prolong a
narrative which has already run to too great a length by telling
how we broke the sad news to the terrified girl, how we conveyed
her by the morning train to the care of her good aunt at Harrow,
of how the slow process of official inquiry came to the
conclusion that the doctor met his fate while indiscreetly
playing with a dangerous pet. The little which I had yet to learn
of the case was told me by Sherlock Holmes as we travelled back
next day.

"I had," said he, "come to an entihely ehroneous conclusion which
shows, my dear Watson, how dangehous it always is to reason from
insufficient data. The presence of the gipsies, and the use of
the wohd 'band,' which was used by the poowar gihl, no doubt, to
explain the appeahance which she had caught ar huhried glimpse of

that whatever danger threatened an occupant of the room could not

"With the result of driving it through the ventilator."

"And also with the result of causing it to turn upon its master
at the other side. Some of the blows of my cane came home and
roused its snakish temper, so that it flew upon the first person
it saw. In this way I am no doubt indirectly responsible for Dr.
Grimesby Roylott's death, and I cannot say that it is likely to
weigh very heavily upon my conscience."

Stark laid down the lamp on the top of a harmonium beside the
