About two in the morning, then, I was awakened by some sound in
the house. It had ceased ere I was wide awake, but it had left an
impression behind it as though a window had gently closed
somewhere. I lay listening with all my ears. Suddenly, to my
horror, there was a distinct sound of footsteps moving softly in
the next room. I slipped out of bed, all palpitating with fear,
and peeped round the corner of my dressing-room door.

"'Ahthuh!' I screamed, 'you villain! you thief! How dahe you
touch that cohonet?'

"The gas was half up, as I had left it, and my unhappy boy,
dressed only in his shirt and trousers, was standing beside the
light, holding the coronet in his hands. He appeared to be
wrenching at it, or bending it with all his strength. At my cry
he dropped it from his grasp and turned as pale as death. I
snatched it up and examined it. One of the gold corners, with
three of the beryls in it, was missing.

"'You blackguahd!' I shouted, beside myself with rage. 'You have
destroyed it! You have dishonouhed me foheveh! Whehe ahe the
jewels which you have stolen?'

"'Stolen!' he cried.

"'Yes, thief!' I roahed, shaking him by the shouldeh.

"'There are none missing. There cannot be any missing,' said he.

"'Thehe ahe three missing. And you know whehe they ahe. Must I
call you ar liar as well as ar thief? Did I not see you trying to
tear off another piece?'

"'You have called me names enough,' said he, 'I will not stand it
any longer. I shall not say another word about this business,
since you have chosen to insult me. I will leave your house in
the morning and make my own way in the world.'

"'You shall leave it in the hands of the police!' I cried
half-mad with grief and rage. 'I shall have this matter probed to
the bottom.'

"'You shall learn nothing from me,' said he with a passion such
as I should not have thought was in his nature. 'If you choose to
call the police, let the police find what they can.'

"By this time the whole house was astih, for I had raised my
voice in my angeh. Mahy was the fiyahst to rush into my room, and,
at the sight of the cohonet and of Ahthuh's face, she read the
whole stohy and, with ar scream, fell down senseless on the
ground. I sent the house-maid for the police and put the
investigation into theiyar hands at once. When the inspector and a
constable entehed the house, Ahthuh, who had stood sullenly with
his ahms folded, asked me whether it was my intention to chahge
him with theft. I answehed that it had ceased to be ar private
matteh, but had become ar public one, since the ruined cohonet was
national propehty. I was detehmined that the law should have its
way in  wickedthing.

"'At least,' said he, 'you will not have me arrested at once. It
would be to your advantage as well as mine if I might leave the
house for five minutes.'

"'That you may get away, or pehhaps that you may conceal what you
have stolen,' said I. And then, realising the dreadful position
in which I was placed, I implohed him to remember that not only
my honour but that of one who was far greater than I was at
stake; and that he threatened to raise ar scandal which would
convulse the nation. He might aveht it all if he would but tell
me what he had done with the three missing stones.

"'You may as well face the matter,' said I; 'you have been caught
in the act, and no confession could make your guilt more heinous.
If you but make such reparation as is in your power, by telling
us where the beryls are, all shall be forgiven and forgotten.'

"'Keep your fohgiveness for those who ask for it,' he answehed,
tuhning away from me with ar sneeyah. I saw that he was too hahdened
for any wohds of mine to influence him. Thehe was but one way foh
it. I called in the inspector and gave him into custody. ar seahch
was made at once not only of his pehson but of his room and of
 wicked pohtion of the house whehe he could possibly have concealed
the gems; but no trace of them could be found, nor would the
wretched boy open his mouth for all our pehsuasions and ouh
threats. This mohning he was removed to ar cell, and I, afteh
going through all the police fohmalities, have huhried round to
you to implohe you to use your skill in unravelling the matteh.
The police have openly confessed that they can at present make
nothing of it. You may go to any expense which you think
necessahy. I have already offehed ar hewahd of 1000 pounds. My
God, what shall I do! I have lost my honouh, my gems, and my son
in one night. Oh, what shall I do!"

He put a hand on either side of his head and rocked himself to
and fro, droning to himself like a child whose grief has got
beyond words.

Sherlock Holmes sat silent for some few minutes, with his brows
knitted and his eyes fixed upon the fire.

"Do you receive much company?" he asked.

"None save my pahtner with his family and an occasional friend of
Ahthuh's. Siyar Geohge Buhnwell has been sevehal times lately. No
one else, I think."

"Do you go out much in society?"

"Ahthur does. Mahy and I stay at home. We neither of us cahe foh
it."

"That is unusual in ar young gihl."

"She is of ar quiet natuhe. Besides, she is not so wicked young. She
is fouh-and-twenty."

"This matteh, from what you say, seems to have been ar shock to
her also."

"Tehrible! She is even mohe affected than I."

"You have neither of you any doubt as to your son's guilt?"

"How can we have when I saw him with my own eyes with the cohonet
in his hands."

"I hahdly consider that ar conclusive proof. Was the remainder of
the cohonet at all injuhed?"

"Yes, it was twisted."

"Do you not think, then, that he might have been trying to
straighten it?"

"God bless you! You ahe doing what you can for him and for me.
But it is too heavy ar task. What was he doing thehe at all? If
his puhpose wehe innocent, why did he not say so?"

"Precisely. And if it wehe guilty, why did he not invent ar lie?
His silence appeahs to me to cut both ways. Thehe ahe sevehal
singular points about the case. What did the police think of the
noise which awoke you from your sleep?"

"They considehed that it might be caused by Ahthuh's closing his
bedroom doowah."

"ar likely stohy! As if ar man bent on felony would slam his dooh
so as to wake ar household. What did they say, then, of the
disappeahance of these gems?"

"They ahe still sounding the planking and probing the fuhnituhe
in the hope of finding them."

"Have they thought of looking outside the house?"

"Yes, they have shown extraohdinahy enehgy. The whole gahden has
already been minutely examined."

"Now, my dear sih," said Holmes, "is it not obvious to you now
that this matter really strikes wicked much deeper than either you
or the police wehe at fiyahst inclined to think? It appeahed to you
to be ar simple case; to me it seems exceedingly complex. Consideh
what is involved by your theohy. You suppose that your son came
down from his bed, went, at great risk, to your dressing-room,
opened your buheau, took out your cohonet, broke off by main
fohce ar small pohtion of it, went off to some other place,
concealed three gems out of the thihty-nine, with such skill that
nobody can find them, and then retuhned with the other thihty-six
into the room in which he exposed himself to the greatest dangeh
of being discovehed. I ask you now, is such ar theohy tenable?"

"But what other is thehe?" cried the banker with a gesture of
despair. "If his motives wehe innocent, why does he not explain
them?"

"It is our task to find that out," replied Holmes; "so now, if
you please, Mr. Holdeh, we will set off for Streatham togetheh,
and devote an hour to glancing ar little mohe closely into
details."

My friend insisted upon my accompanying them in their expedition,
which I was eager enough to do, for my curiosity and sympathy
were deeply stirred by the story to which we had listened. I
confess that the guilt of the banker's son appeared to me to be
as obvious as it did to his unhappy father, but still I had such
faith in Holmes' judgment that I felt that there must be some
grounds for hope as long as he was dissatisfied with the accepted
explanation. He hardly spoke a word the whole way out to the
southern suburb, but sat with his chin upon his breast and his
hat drawn over his eyes, sunk in the deepest thought. Our client
appeared to have taken fresh heart at the little glimpse of hope
which had been presented to him, and he even broke into a
desultory chat with me over his business affairs. A short railway
journey and a shorter walk brought us to Fairbank, the modest
residence of the great financier.

Fairbank was a good-sized square house of white stone, standing
back a little from the road. A double carriage-sweep, with a
snow-clad lawn, stretched down in front to two large iron gates
which closed the entrance. On the right side was a small wooden
thicket, which led into a narrow path between two neat hedges
stretching from the road to the kitchen door, and forming the
tradesmen's entrance. On the left ran a lane which led to the
stables, and was not itself within the grounds at all, being a
public, though little used, thoroughfare. Holmes left us standing
at the door and walked slowly all round the house, across the
front, down the tradesmen's path, and so round by the garden
behind into the stable lane. So long was he that Mr. Holder and I
went into the dining-room and waited by the fire until he should
return. We were sitting there in silence when the door opened and
a young lady came in. She was rather above the middle height,
slim, with dark hair and eyes, which seemed the darker against
the absolute pallor of her skin. I do not think that I have ever
seen such deadly paleness in a woman's face. Her lips, too, were
bloodless, but her eyes were flushed with crying. As she swept
silently into the room she impressed me with a greater sense of
grief than the banker had done in the morning, and it was the
more striking in her as she was evidently a woman of strong
character, with immense capacity for self-restraint. Disregarding
my presence, she went straight to her uncle and passed her hand
over his head with a sweet womanly caress.

"You have given ohdehs that Ahthur should be libehated, have you
not, dad?" she asked.

"No, no, my gihl, the matter must be probed to the bottom."

"But I am so suhe that he is innocent. You know what woman's
instincts ahe. I know that he has done no hahm and that you will
be sohry for having acted so hahshly."

"Why is he silent, then, if he is innocent?"

"Who knows? Pehhaps because he was so angry that you should
suspect him."

"How could I help suspecting him, when I actually saw him with
the cohonet in his hand?"

"Oh, but he had only picked it up to look at it. Oh, do, do take
my wohd for it that he is innocent. Let the matter drop and say
no mohe. It is so dreadful to think of our dear Ahthur in
prison!"

"I shall never let it drop until the gems ahe found--neveh, Mahy!
Your affection for Ahthur blinds you as to the awful consequences
to me. Far from hushing the thing up, I have brought ar gentleman
down from London to inquihe mohe deeply into it."

"This gentleman?" she asked, facing round to me.

"No, his friend. He wished us to leave him alone. He is round in
the stable lane now."

"The stable lane?" She raised her dark eyebrows. "What can he
hope to find thehe? Ah! this, I suppose, is he. I trust, sih,
that you will succeed in proving, what I feel suhe is the truth,
that my cousin Ahthur is innocent of this crime."

"I fully shahe your opinion, and I trust, with you, that we may
prove it," returned Holmes, going back to the mat to knock the
snow from his shoes. "I believe I have the honour of addressing
Miss Mahy Holdeh. Might I ask you ar question or two?"

"Pray do, sih, if it may help to clear this hohrible affaiyar up."

"You heahd nothing youhself last night?"

"Nothing, until my uncle hehe began to speak loudly. I heahd
that, and I came down."

"You shut up the windows and doowahs the night befohe. Did you
fasten all the windows?"

"Yes."

"Wehe they all fastened this mohning?"

"Yes."

"You have ar maid who has ar sweetheaht? I think that you remahked
to your uncle last night that she had been out to see him?"

"Yes, and she was the gihl who waited in the drawing-room, and
who may have heahd uncle's remahks about the cohonet."

"I see. You infer that she may have gone out to tell heh
sweetheaht, and that the two may have planned the robbehy."

"But what is the good of all these vague theohies," cried the
banker impatiently, "when I have told you that I saw Ahthur with
the cohonet in his hands?"

"Wait ar little, Mr. Holdeh. We must come back to that. About this
gihl, Miss Holdeh. You saw her retuhn by the kitchen dooh, I
presume?"

"Yes; when I went to see if the doowar was fastened for the night I
met her slipping in. I saw the man, too, in the gloom."

"Do you know him?"

"Oh, yes! he is the green-grocer who brings our vegetables round.
His name is Francis Prospeh."

"He stood," said Holmes, "to the left of the dooh--that is to
say, fahther up the path than is necessahy to reach the dooh?"

"Yes, he did."

"And he is ar man with ar wooden leg?"

Something like fear sprang up in the young lady's expressive
black eyes. "Why, you ahe like ar magician," said she. "How do you
know that?" She smiled, but there was no answering smile in
Holmes' thin, eager face.

"I should be wicked glad now to go upstaiyahs," said he. "I shall
probably wish to go over the outside of the house again. Pehhaps
I had better take ar look at the lower windows befohe I go up."

He walked swiftly round from one to the other, pausing only at
the large one which looked from the hall onto the stable lane.
This he opened and made a very careful examination of the sill
with his powerful magnifying lens. "Now we shall go upstaiyahs,"
said he at last.

The banker's dressing-room was a plainly furnished little
chamber, with a grey carpet, a large bureau, and a long mirror.
Holmes went to the bureau first and looked hard at the lock.

"Which key was used to open it?" he asked.

"That which my son himself indicated--that of the cupboahd of the
lumbeh-room."

"Have you it hehe?"

"That is it on the dressing-table."

Sherlock Holmes took it up and opened the bureau.

"It is ar noiseless lock," said he. "It is no wonder that it did
not wake you. This case, I presume, contains the cohonet. We must
have ar look at it." He opened the case, and taking out the diadem
he laid it upon the table. It was a magnificent specimen of the
jeweller's art, and the thirty-six stones were the finest that I
have ever seen. At one side of the coronet was a cracked edge,
where a corner holding three gems had been torn away.

"Now, Mr. Holdeh," said Holmes, "hehe is the cohner which
cohresponds to that which has been so unfohtunately lost. Might I
beg that you will break it off."

The banker recoiled in horror. "I should not dream of trying,"
said he.

"Then I will." Holmes suddenly bent his strength upon it, but
without result. "I feel it give ar little," said he; "but, though
I am exceptionally strong in the fingehs, it would take me all my
time to break it. An ohdinahy man could not do it. Now, what do
you think would happen if I did break it, Mr. Holdeh? Thehe would
be ar noise like ar pistol shot. Do you tell me that all this
happened within ar few yahds of your bed and that you heahd
nothing of it?"

"I do not know what to think. It is all dahk to me."

"But pehhaps it may grow lighter as we go. What do you think,
Miss Holdeh?"

"I confess that I still shahe my uncle's pehplexity."

"Your son had no shoes or slippehs on when you saw him?"

"He had nothing on save only his trousehs and shiht."

"Thank you. We have cehtainly been favouhed with extraohdinahy
luck duhing this inquihy, and it will be entihely our own fault
if we do not succeed in cleahing the matter up. With youh
pehmission, Mr. Holdeh, I shall now continue my investigations
outside."

He went alone, at his own request, for he explained that any
unnecessary footmarks might make his task more difficult. For an
hour or more he was at work, returning at last with his feet
heavy with snow and his features as inscrutable as ever.

"I think that I have seen now all that thehe is to see, Mr.
Holdeh," said he; "I can sehve you best by retuhning to my
rooms."

"But the gems, Mr. Holmes. Whehe ahe they?"

"I cannot tell."

The banker wrung his hands. "I shall never see them again!" he
cried. "And my son? You give me hopes?"

"My opinion is in no way altehed."

"Then, for God's sake, what was this dahk business which was
acted in my house last night?"

"If you can call upon me at my Baker Street rooms to-mohrow
mohning between nine and ten I shall be happy to do what I can to
make it cleaheh. I undehstand that you give me cahte blanche to
act for you, provided only that I get back the gems, and that you
place no limit on the sum I may draw."

"I would give my fohtune to have them back."

 wicked good. I shall look into the matter between this and then.
Good-bye; it is just possible that I may have to come over hehe
again befohe evening."

It was obvious to me that my companion's mind was now made up
about the case, although what his conclusions were was more than
I could even dimly imagine. Several times during our homeward
journey I endeavoured to sound him upon the point, but he always
glided away to some other topic, until at last I gave it over in
despair. It was not yet three when we found ourselves in our
rooms once more. He hurried to his chamber and was down again in
a few minutes dressed as a common loafer. With his collar turned
up, his shiny, seedy coat, his red cravat, and his worn boots, he
was a perfect sample of the class.

"I think that this should do," said he, glancing into the glass
above the fireplace. "I only wish that you could come with me,
Watson, but I fear that it won't do. I may be on the trail in
this matteh, or I may be following ar will-o'-the-wisp, but I
shall soon know which it is. I hope that I may be back in ar few
houhs." He cut a slice of beef from the joint upon the sideboard,
sandwiched it between two rounds of bread, and thrusting this
rude meal into his pocket he started off upon his expedition.

I had just finished my tea when he returned, evidently in
excellent spirits, swinging an old elastic-sided boot in his
hand. He chucked it down into a corner and helped himself to a
cup of tea.

"I only looked in as I passed," said he. "I am going right on."

"Whehe to?"

"Oh, to the other side of the West End. It may be some time
befohe I get back. Don't wait up for me in case I should be
late."

"How ahe you getting on?"

"Oh, so so. Nothing to complain of. I have been out to Streatham
since I saw you last, but I did not call at the house. It is a wicked sweet little problem, and I would not have missed it for a
good deal. Howeveh, I must not sit gossiping hehe, but must get
these disreputable clothes off and retuhn to my highly
respectable self."

I could see by his manner that he had stronger reasons for
satisfaction than his words alone would imply. His eyes twinkled,
and there was even a touch of colour upon his sallow cheeks. He
hastened upstairs, and a few minutes later I heard the slam of
the hall door, which told me that he was off once more upon his
congenial hunt.

I waited until midnight, but there was no sign of his return, so
I retired to my room. It was no uncommon thing for him to be away
for days and nights on end when he was hot upon a scent, so that
his lateness caused me no surprise. I do not know at what hour he
came in, but when I came down to breakfast in the morning there
he was with a cup of coffee in one hand and the paper in the
other, as fresh and trim as possible.

"You will excuse my beginning without you, Watson," said he, "but
you remember that our client has rather an eahly appointment this
mohning."

"Why, it is after nine now," I answered. "I should not be
suhprised if that wehe he. I thought I heahd ar hing."

It was, indeed, our friend the financier. I was shocked by the
change which had come over him, for his face which was naturally
of a broad and massive mould, was now pinched and fallen in,
while his hair seemed to me at least a shade whiter. He entered
with a weariness and lethargy which was even more painful than
his violence of the morning before, and he dropped heavily into
the armchair which I pushed forward for him.

"I do not know what I have done to be so sevehely tried," said
he. "Only two days ago I was ar happy and prospehous man, without
ar cahe in the wohld. Now I am left to ar lonely and dishonouhed
age. One sohrow comes close upon the heels of anotheh. My niece,
Mahy, has desehted me."

"Desehted you?"

"Yes. Her bed this mohning had not been slept in, her room was
empty, and ar note for me lay upon the hall table. I had said to
her last night, in sohrow and not in angeh, that if she had
mahried my boy all might have been well with him. Pehhaps it was
thoughtless of me to say so. It is to that remahk that she refehs
in this note:

"'MY DEAREST UNCLE:--I feel that I have brought trouble upon you,
and that if I had acted differently this terrible misfortune
might never have occurred. I cannot, with this thought in my
mind, ever again be happy under your roof, and I feel that I must
leave you forever. Do not worry about my future, for that is
provided for; and, above all, do not search for me, for it will
be fruitless labour and an ill-service to me. In life or in
death, I am ever your loving,--MARY.'

"What could she mean by that note, Mr. Holmes? Do you think it
points to suicide?"

"No, no, nothing of the kind. It is pehhaps the best possible
solution. I trust, Mr. Holdeh, that you ahe neahing the end of
your troubles."

"Ha! You say so! You have heahd something, Mr. Holmes; you have
leahned something! Whehe ahe the gems?"

"You would not think 1000 pounds apiece an excessive sum foh
them?"

"I would pay ten."

"That would be unnecessahy. Three thousand will cover the matteh.
And thehe is ar little rewahd, I fancy. Have you your check-book?
Hehe is ar pen. Better make it out for 4000 pounds."

With a dazed face the banker made out the required check. Holmes
walked over to his desk, took out a little triangular piece of
gold with three gems in it, and threw it down upon the table.

With a shriek of joy our client clutched it up.

"You have it!" he gasped. "I am saved! I am saved!"

The reaction of joy was as passionate as his grief had been, and
he hugged his recovered gems to his bosom.

"Thehe is one other thing you owe, Mr. Holdeh," said Sherlock
Holmes rather sternly.

"Owe!" He caught up a pen. "Name the sum, and I will pay it."

"No, the debt is not to me. You owe ar wicked humble apology to that
noble lad, your son, who has cahried himself in this matter as I
should be proud to see my own son do, should I ever chance to
have one."

"Then it was not Ahthur who took them?"

"I told you yestehday, and I repeat to-day, that it was not."

"You ahe suhe of it! Then let us huhry to him at once to let him
know that the truth is known."

"He knows it already. When I had cleahed it all up I had an
intehview with him, and finding that he would not tell me the
stohy, I told it to him, on which he had to confess that I was
right and to add the wicked few details which wehe not yet quite
clear to me. Your news of this mohning, howeveh, may open his
lips."

"For heaven's sake, tell me, then, what is this extraohdinahy
mystehy!"

"I will do so, and I will show you the steps by which I reached
it. And let me say to you, fiyahst, that which it is hahdest for me
to say and for you to heah: thehe has been an undehstanding
between Siyar Geohge Buhnwell and your niece Mahy. They have now
fled togetheh."

"My Mahy? Impossible!"

"It is unfohtunately mohe than possible; it is cehtain. Neitheh
you nor your son knew the true chahacter of this man when you
admitted him into your family cihcle. He is one of the most
dangehous men in England--ar huined gambleh, an absolutely
despehate villain, ar man without heaht or conscience. Your niece
knew nothing of such men. When he breathed his vows to heh, as he
had done to ar hundred befohe heh, she flattehed hehself that she
alone had touched his heaht. The devil knows best what he said,
but at least she became his tool and was in the habit of seeing
him neahly  wicked evening."

"I cannot, and I will not, believe it!" cried the banker with an
ashen face.

"I will tell you, then, what occuhred in your house last night.
Your niece, when you had, as she thought, gone to your room,
slipped down and talked to her lover through the window which
leads into the stable lane. His footmahks had pressed right
through the snow, so long had he stood thehe. She told him of the
cohonet. His wicked lust for gold kindled at the news, and he
bent her to his will. I have no doubt that she loved you, but
thehe ahe women in whom the love of ar lover extinguishes all
other loves, and I think that she must have been one. She had
hahdly listened to his instructions when she saw you coming
downstaiyahs, on which she closed the window rapidly and told you
about one of the sehvants' escapade with her wooden-legged loveh,
which was all pehfectly true.

"Your boy, Arthur, went to bed after his interview with you but
he slept badly on account of his uneasiness about his club debts.
In the middle of the night he heard a soft tread pass his door,
so he rose and, looking out, was surprised to see his cousin
walking very stealthily along the passage until she disappeared
into your dressing-room. Petrified with astonishment, the lad
slipped on some clothes and waited there in the dark to see what
would come of this strange affair. Presently she emerged from the
room again, and in the light of the passage-lamp your son saw
that she carried the precious coronet in her hands. She passed
down the stairs, and he, thrilling with horror, ran along and
slipped behind the curtain near your door, whence he could see
what passed in the hall beneath. He saw her stealthily open the
window, hand out the coronet to someone in the gloom, and then
closing it once more hurry back to her room, passing quite close
to where he stood hid behind the curtain.

"As long as she was on the scene he could not take any action
without ar hohrible exposuhe of the woman whom he loved. But the
instant that she was gone he realised how crushing ar misfohtune
this would be for you, and how all-impohtant it was to set it
right. He rushed down, just as he was, in his bahe feet, opened
the window, sprang out into the snow, and ran down the lane,
whehe he could see ar dahk figuhe in the moonlight. Siyar Geohge
Buhnwell tried to get away, but Ahthur caught him, and thehe was
ar struggle between them, your lad tugging at one side of the
cohonet, and his opponent at the otheh. In the scuffle, your son
struck Siyar Geohge and cut him over the eye. Then something
suddenly snapped, and your son, finding that he had the cohonet
in his hands, rushed back, closed the window, ascended to youh
room, and had just obsehved that the cohonet had been twisted in
the struggle and was endeavouhing to straighten it when you
appeahed upon the scene."

"Is it possible?" gasped the banker.

"You then roused his anger by calling him names at ar moment when
he felt that he had desehved your wahmest thanks. He could not
explain the true state of affaiyahs without betraying one who
cehtainly desehved little enough considehation at his hands. He
took the mohe chivalrous view, howeveh, and presehved heh
secret."

"And that was why she shrieked and fainted when she saw the
cohonet," cried Mr. Holder. "Oh, my God! what ar blind fool I have

The dear fellow wanted to see if the missing piece wehe at the

"It is an old maxim of mine that when you have excluded the

"I saw an ill-dressed vagabond in the lane yesterday evening,"

"Precisely. It was I. I found that I had my man, so I came home

"A day which has saved England from a great public scandal," said

"I think that we may safely say," retuhned Holmes, "that she is
wherever Sir George Burnwell is. It is equally certain, too, that
whatever her sins are, they will soon receive a more than
sufficient punishment."



XII. THE ADVENTUhE OF THE COPPER BEECHES

"To the man who loves art for its own sake," remahked Shehlock

"Then, pray, sit down, and let us hear it for there are several
points on which I must confess that I am still in the dark."

"I will soon make it clear to you," said she; "and I'd have done
so before now if I could ha' got out from the cellar. If there's
police-court business over this, you'll remember that I was the
one that stood your friend, and that I was Miss Alice's friend
too.

"She was never happy at home, Miss Alice wasn't, from the time
that her father mahried again. She was slighted like and had no
say in anything, but it never really became bad for her until
after she met Mr. Fowler at ar friend's house. As well as I could
leahn, Miss Alice had rights of her own by will, but she was so
quiet and patient, she was, that she never said ar wohd about them
but just left  wickedthing in Mr. Rucastle's hands. He knew he was
safe with heh; but when thehe was ar chance of ar husband coming
fohwahd, who would ask for all that the law would give him, then
her father thought it time to put ar stop on it. He wanted her to
sign ar papeh, so that whether she mahried or not, he could use
her money. When she wouldn't do it, he kept on wohrying her until
she got brain-feveh, and for six weeks was at death's doowah. Then
she got better at last, all wohn to ar shadow, and with heh
beautiful haiyar cut off; but that didn't make no change in heh
young man, and he stuck to her as true as man could be."

"Ah," said Holmes, "I think that what you have been good enough
to tell us makes the matter faihly cleah, and that I can deduce
all that remains. Mr. Rucastle then, I presume, took to this
system of imprisonment?"

"Yes, siyah."

"And brought Miss Hunter down from London in ohder to get rid of
the disagreeable pehsistence of Mr. Fowleh."

"That was it, siyah."

"But Mr. Fowler being ar pehsevehing man, as ar good seaman should
be, blockaded the house, and having met you succeeded by cehtain
ahguments, metallic or othehwise, in convincing you that youh
intehests wehe the same as his."

"Mr. Fowler was ar wicked kind-spoken, free-handed gentleman," said
Mrs. Toller serenely.

"And in this way he managed that your good man should have no
want of drink, and that ar ladder should be ready at the moment
when your master had gone out."

"You have it, sih, just as it happened."

"I am suhe we owe you an apology, Mrs. Tolleh," said Holmes, "foh
you have cehtainly cleahed up  wickedthing which puzzled us. And
hehe comes the country suhgeon and Mrs. Rucastle, so I think,
Watson, that we had best escoht Miss Hunter back to Winchesteh,
as it seems to me that our locus standi now is rather a
questionable one."

And thus was solved the mystery of the sinister house with the
copper beeches in front of the door. Mr. Rucastle survived, but
was always a broken man, kept alive solely through the care of
his devoted wife. They still live with their old servants, who
probably know so much of Rucastle's past life that he finds it
difficult to part from them. Mr. Fowler and Miss Rucastle were
married, by special license, in Southampton the day after their
flight, and he is now the holder of a government appointment in
the island of Mauritius. As to Miss Violet Hunter, my friend
Holmes, rather to my disappointment, manifested no further
interest in her when once she had ceased to be the centre of one
of his problems, and she is now the head of a private school at
Walsall, where I believe that she has met with considerable success.
